Wills Must End in a Necessary Will to Avoid Infinite Regress
Razi proves that wills terminate in a necessary will to avoid infinite regress, affirming God's decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that wills must terminate in a necessary will, and how this supports the doctrine that all things are from God's decree without negating human choice.

Core Idea
Wills cannot regress infinitely; they must end in God's necessary will.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a proof that the chain of wills cannot go on forever; it must culminate in a will that is necessary and uncaused. He then clarifies that this does not eliminate human choice, because choice itself operates through powers granted by God. The only way to truly deny divine decree is to reject the principle that nothing effects except God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the infinite regress problem
Read the passage and identify why infinite regress of wills is impossible. Notice how Razi states that wills must end in a necessary will.
The Arabic term for infinite regress is 'tasalsul'.
2
Examine the two interpretations of God's decree
Razi presents two ways: God's decree can be directly without mediation or through mediation. Find these in the passage and note their implications.
3
Grasp the relationship between divine decree and human choice
Razi argues that the second interpretation does not contradict human choice. Reflect on how choice is defined as action through mediated power or will, all from God.
This leads to the principle 'la mu'aththira illa Allah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الإرادات تنتهى إلى إرادة ضرورية دفعا للتسلسل وذلك يوجب الاعتراف باستناد الكل إلى قضاء الله تعالى وقدره . أقول : قبل استناد الكل إلى قضاء الله تعالى وقدره إما أن يكون بلا توسط في إيجاد الشيء أو يكون بتوسط، والأول يقتضيه انتهاء الإرادات إلى إرادته ، والثانى لا ينافى القول بالاختيار ، فإن الاختيار هو الإيجاد بتوسط قدرة أو إرادة سواء كانت تلك القدرة والإرادة من فعل الله تعالى بلا توسط أو بتوسط شيء آخر ، فإذا من قضاء الله تعالى وقدره وقوع بعض الأفعال تابعا لاختيار فاعله ، ولا يندفع هذا إلا بإقامة البرهان على أنه لا مؤثر إلا الله تعالى
Issue: Wills end at a necessary will, to avoid infinite regress, and that necessitates acknowledging that all is based on God Almighty's decree and predestination. I say: Regarding all being based on God Almighty's decree and predestination, either it is without mediation in bringing the thing into existence, or it is with mediation. The first is necessitated by wills ending at His will. The second does not contradict the statement of choice (al-ikhtiyar), for choice is bringing into existence through the mediation of power or will, whether that power and will are from God Almighty's act without mediation or through the mediation of something else. So, from God's decree and predestination is the occurrence of some acts following the choice of their agent, and this is not repelled except by establishing the proof that there is no effecter except God Almighty.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the necessity of a first will to avoid infinite regress (tasalsul). Since wills are contingent, they cannot regress infinitely; there must be a necessary will that stops the regress. Razi then comments that acknowledging all acts as from God's decree can be understood in two ways: either God directly creates all things without mediation, or He creates through mediators (like human power and will). The first is implied by wills ending in God's will. The second does not contradict the concept of human choice (ikhtiyar), because choice itself involves action through mediating power or will, which are also from God. Thus, even acts that follow human choice are still under God's decree. The only way to deny this is to prove that nothing effects except God (la mu'aththira illa Allah).

Key Terms
إرادة ضرورية
تسلسل
قضاء
قدر
اختيار
لا مؤثر إلا الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If humans have free choice, then their actions are not from God's decree.
Correction: Razi explains that choice itself is mediated by powers from God, so even chosen acts are under God's decree.

Checkpoint
Why must wills end in a necessary will according to Razi?
A) Because every will requires another will to cause it
B) To avoid infinite regress
C) Because God's will is the only will
D) Because human will is not real

Key Takeaway
Wills must end in God's necessary will, and even human choice is encompassed by divine decree.

Four Possibilities and the First Two Views
Razi introduces the four logical possibilities and explains the first two views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical possibilities for the temporality of bodies and the views of the majority religious communities and the Peripatetic philosophers.

Core Idea
The possible views on creation are exactly four, and only two are widely held.

Razi begins by setting out the logical framework for discussing the creation of bodies. He identifies four and only four possible positions. The first is the view of most religious communities; the second is that of the Aristotelian philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the four options
The passage opens with the statement that the possible views are exactly four. Read carefully the description of each.
Notice the phrasing: 'إما أن يكون... أو... أو... أو بالعكس'.
2
Understand the first option and its proponents
The first view (originated in essence and attributes) is held by the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Magians.
3
Examine the second option in detail
The second view (eternal in both) is attributed to Aristotle and his followers, who held the heavens eternal except for movements and positions, and elements eternal as individuals.
Note the distinction between the essences and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثانى فى العوارض) مسألة : اختلف أهل العالم في حدوث الأجسام ) والوجوه الممكنة فيه لا تزيد على أربعة . فإنه إما أن يكون محدث الذات والصفات . أو قديم الذات والصفات . أو قديم الذات محدث الصفات . أو بالعكس . أما الأول: فهو قول الجمهور من المسلمين والنصارى واليهود والمجوس . وأما الثاني: فهو قول أرسطاطاليس وثا فرسطس وثاوم سطليوس وبرقلس ومن المتأخرين أبى نصر الفارابي وألى على ابن سينا، وعدهم أن السموات قديمة بذاتها وصفاتها المعينة إلا الحركات والأوضاع ، فإن كل واحد منها حادث ومسبوق آخر لا إلى أول وأما العناصر والهيولى فهي قديمة بشخصها والجسمية قديمة بنوعها وسائر الصور قديمة بجنسها أى كانت قبل كل صورة صورة أخرى لا إلى بداية. وأما الثالث: فهو قول الفلاسفة الذين كانوا قبل أرسطاطاليس بالزمان كتاليس وانكسافورس وسقراط وقول جميع الثنوية كالمانوية والديصانية والمرقونية والمساهانية ، ثم هؤلاء فريقان . الفرقة الأولى : الذين زعموا أن تلك المادة جسم ثم زعم تاليس أنه الماء لأنه قابل لكل الصور وزعم إنه إذا اجمد صار أرضا وإذا لطف صار هواء و من صفوة الهواء تكونت النار ومن الدخان تكونت السموات ، ويقال إنه أخذه من التوراة لأنه جاء فى السفر الأول منها أن الله تعالى خلق جوهرا فنظر إليه بنظر الهيبة فذابت أجزاؤه فصارت ماء ثم ارتفع منه بخار كالدخان فخلق منه السمرات فظهر على وجه الماء زید فخاق منه الأرض ثم أرساها بالجبال ، وزعم انكسم ا ينس أنه الهواء وكون النار من لطافته والماء والأرض من كثافته ، وزعم أبر بليطس أنه النار وكون الأشياء عنها بالتكاتف ، وآخرون قالوا إنه الأرض وكون الأشياء عنه بالتلطيف ، وآخرون أنه البخار وكون الهواء والعار عنه بالتلطيف والماء والأرض بالتكثيف ، و عن انكساغورس أنه الخليط الذى لا نهاية له وهو أجسام غير متناهية ومنه من كل نوع أجزاء صغيرة متلا لقية : أجزاء على طبيعة الخبز وأجزاء على طبيعة اللحم فإذا اجتمع من تلك الأجزاء شيء كثير صار بحيث يحس ويرى ظن أنه حدث ، وهذا القائل بنى على هذا المذهب إنكار المزاج والاستحالة ، وقال بالكمون والظهور ، وزعم بعض هؤلاء أن ذلك الخليط كان ساكنا فى الأزل ثم إن الله تعالى حركة فكون منه هذا العالم، فزهم ديمقراطيس أن أصل العالم أجزاء صغيرة كرية الشكل قابلة للقسمة الوهمية دون القسمة الانفكاكية متحركة لذاتها حركات دائمة ، ثم اتفق فى تلك الأجزاء أن تصادفت على وجه خاص فحصل من تصادفها على ذلك الوجه هذا العالم على هذا الشكل فحدثت السموات والعناصر ، ثم حدث من الحركات السماوية امتزاجات هذه العناصر ومنها هذه المركبات ، وزعمت الثنوية أن أصل العالم هو النور والمظلمة (1) أقول: صاحب الملل والنحل نقل عن ثماليس الملطى أنه قال المبدأ الأول أبدع العنصر الذي فيه صور الموجودات والمعدومات كلها فانبحث من كل صورة موجود فى العالم على المال الذي في العنصر الأول ، فمحل الصور ومنبع الموجودات هو ذات العنصر ، وما من موجود فى العالم العقلى والعالم الحسى إلا وفي ذات المنصر صورة ومثال منه قال : ويتصور العامة أن الصور والمعلومات فى ذات المبدأ الأول لا بل هي في مبدعه وهي تعالى بوحدانيته أن يوصف بما يوصف به مبدعه ، ثم قال ومن العجب أنه نقل عنه أن المبدع الأول هو الماء منه أبدع الجواهر كلها من السماء والأرض وما بينهما ، فذكر أن من جموده تكوين الأرض ومن انخلا له تكوين الهواء، و من صفوة الهواء تكونت الدار ، ومن الدخان والأبخرة تكونت السماء فدارت حول المركز دوران المسبب على سبيه بالشوق الحاصل ، وفي الأخير قال وفي التوراة فى السفر الأول جوهر خلقه الله ثم نظر إليه إلى آخره، قال وكان ثاليس الملطى إنما تلقى مذهبه من هذه المشكاة النبوية ، قال والماء على القول الثانى شديد الشبه بالماء الذي عليه العرش وكان عرشه على الماء، وأما انكياس الملطى نقل عن مذهبه في التوحيد وخلق الأشياء ثم
The second consideration on accidents. Proposition: The people of the world differed on the temporality (حدوث) of bodies. And the possible aspects therein do not exceed four. For it is either: (1) temporally originated in essence and attributes; or (2) eternal in essence and attributes; or (3) eternal in essence, temporally originated in attributes; or (4) the opposite. As for the first: it is the statement of the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Magians. As for the second: it is the statement of Aristotle, Theophrastus, Themistius, Proclus, and among the later ones Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. They hold that the heavens are eternal in their essence and specific attributes except for movements and positions, for each of them is temporally originated and preceded by another without a first; and the elements and hyle are eternal in their individuals, and corporeality is eternal in its species, and all [other] forms are eternal in their genus, i.e., before every form there is another form without beginning. As for the third: it is the statement of the philosophers who were before Aristotle in time, such as Thales, Anaxagoras, Socrates, and the statement of all the dualists such as the Manichaeans, the Daysanites, the Marcionites, and the 'Masahaniyya' [?]. Then these are two groups. The first group: those who claimed that that matter is a body. Then Thales claimed that it is water because it is receptive of all forms. He claimed that when it solidifies it becomes earth, when it rarefies it becomes air, from the purest of air fire is formed, and from smoke the heavens are formed. It is said that he took this from the Torah, because in the first book thereof it is stated that God created a substance, He gazed at it with a gaze of awe, so its parts melted and became water, then vapor like smoke rose from it, from which He created the heavens; then foam appeared on the water's surface, from which He created the earth, then He fixed it with mountains. And Anaximenes claimed that it is air, and fire comes from its rarefaction, water and earth from its condensation. And Archelaus claimed that it is fire, and things come from it by cohesion. Others said it is earth, and things come from it by rarefaction. Others said it is vapor, and air and fire come from it by rarefaction, water and earth by condensation. And Anaxagoras said it is the infinite mixture, which is infinite bodies, and from it of every kind there are small parts that meet: parts of the nature of bread, parts of the nature of flesh. When many of those parts come together, it becomes such that it is sensed and seen, and it is thought to have come into being. This proponent built upon this doctrine the denial of mixture and transformation, and posited latency and manifestation. Some of these claimed that that mixture was quiescent in pre-eternity, then God moved it and formed this world from it. And Democritus claimed that the origin of the world is small spherical parts, divisible in estimation but not divisible by separation, moving by themselves with perpetual motions. Then these parts happened to collide in a particular manner, and from their collision in that manner this world came to be in this shape, so the heavens and elements arose, then from the celestial movements mixtures of these elements came about, and from them these composites. And the dualists claimed that the origin of the world is light and darkness. (1) I say: The author of al-Milal wa al-Nihal reported from Thales of Miletus that he said: The First Principle created the element which contains the forms of all existents and non-existents, and from every form an existent in the world emanates in accordance with the form in the first element. So the locus of forms and the source of existents is the essence of the element, and there is no existent in the intelligible world and the sensible world except that in the essence of the element there is a form and a likeness of it. He said: The common people imagine that the forms and cognitions are in the First Principle, but rather they are in His created (element). And He, exalted, is by His unity too exalted to be described by what His created is described with. Then he said: And it is amazing that it is reported from him that the First Principle is water, from which He created all the jewels/substances of heaven, earth, and what is between them. He mentioned that from its freezing comes the formation of earth, from its evaporation comes the formation of air, from the purest of air the abode [heaven?] was formed, and from smoke and vapors heaven was formed, and it revolved around the center in the manner of a waterwheel, due to the desire that occurs. And at the end he said: In the Torah, in the first book, there is a substance that God created, then He looked at it etc. He said: Thales of Miletus only received his doctrine from this prophetic niche. He said: And water according to the second statement is very similar to the water upon which the Throne was, and His Throne was upon water. And as for Anaximenes of Miletus, it is reported from him regarding his doctrine on tawhid and the creation of things, then...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This extensive passage is the beginning of the second consideration (النظر الثاني) on accidents, specifically dealing with the differing opinions on the temporal origination (حدوث) of bodies. It is a classification passage. Razi first states that the possible views are exactly four: 1) originated in essence and attributes; 2) eternal in both; 3) eternal in essence but originated in attributes; 4) the opposite (originated in essence but eternal in attributes). He then describes the first view as that of the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Magians. The second view is attributed to Aristotle and his followers (Theophrastus, Themistius, Proclus, and later al-Farabi and Ibn Sina). Their view is that the heavens are eternal in essence and in their specific attributes, except for movements and positions which are temporally originated in an infinite series. The elements and hyle are eternal as individuals, corporeality is eternal as a species, and all other forms are eternal as a genus, meaning before every form there is another form without beginning. The third view is that of the pre-Aristotelian philosophers (Thales, Anaxagoras, Socrates) and the dualists (Manichaeans, Daysanites, Marcionites, Masahaniyya). Razi then splits these into two groups. The first group (الفرقة الأولى) are those who say that the matter (المادة) is a body. Under this, he enumerates various ancient Greek philosophers and their views on the material principle: Thales (water), Anaximenes (air), Archelaus (fire), and others (earth, vapor). He also mentions Anaxagoras' theory of infinite mixture with seeds of all kinds, leading to the denial of mixture and transformation and the assertion of latency and manifestation (الكمون والظهور). Some of Anaxagoras' followers said the mixture was originally static and then God moved it. Then Democritus' atomic theory is described: small spherical parts (atoms) that are indivisible in actual separation but divisible in estimation, moving eternally, and their random collisions form the world. Finally, the dualists (الثنوية) are said to believe that the origin is light and darkness. The passage concludes with a lengthy footnote (marked (1)) which appears to be a quotation from al-Shahrastani's 'al-Milal wa al-Nihal' discussing Thales of Miletus in more detail, including a link to the Torah. This footnote comments on Thales' view that the First Principle is water and notes a similarity to the Qur'anic mention of water and the Throne. The text of the footnote is incomplete at the end.

Key Terms

قدم
جسم

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four possible positions mentioned by Razi?
A) Temporally originated in essence and attributes
B) Eternal in essence and attributes
C) Eternal in essence, temporally originated in attributes

Key Takeaway
The possible views on the creation of bodies are exactly four, and the first two are held by the majority of religious communities and the Peripatetic philosophers respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the first view (temporal origination of both essence and attributes), do you truly believe that everything about yourself—your very essence and every attribute—is contingent and created by Allah? If so, why do you sometimes act as if your attributes (like knowledge or strength) are self-sufficient? How can you cultivate a deeper sense of contingency today?
?
Razi mentions that the second view (eternal essence and attributes) is held by great philosophers. But have you ever felt tempted to think that some part of the universe is eternal alongside Allah? Examine your heart: Do you ever rely on the 'eternity' of natural laws or material causes as if they operate independently of Allah's constant creation? What would change if you truly internalized that only Allah is eternal?
?
The passage forces you to choose among four logical possibilities. Which one do you actually live by? Not just intellectually, but in your daily reliance on Allah vs. reliance on created causes? If you find yourself slipping into the second view in practice (acting as if causes are self-sufficient), what concrete step will you take today to reaffirm your belief in Allah's absolute origination?

Pre-Aristotelian Philosophers and Their Material Principles
Razi enumerates the pre-Aristotelian philosophers who held that matter is a body and their proposed material principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the views of pre-Aristotelian philosophers, including Thales, Anaximenes, Archelaus, and others, on the material principle of the universe.

Core Idea
Before Aristotle, philosophers sought a single material principle from which everything arises.

Razi continues with the third possible position, that bodies are eternal in essence but temporally originated in attributes. He divides its proponents into two groups. The first group holds that matter is a body, and he lists several ancient Greek philosophers who each identified a different material principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups within the third view
The third view includes those who say matter is a body (group one) and those who say matter is not a body (group two, not detailed here).
2
Read about Thales and his water theory
Thales claimed water is the fundamental substance because it is receptive of all forms, and he linked it to a story from the Torah.
Note the mention of the Torah and the creation narrative.
3
Explore other philosophers: Anaximenes, Archelaus, and others
Anaximenes proposed air, Archelaus proposed fire, and others proposed earth or vapor. Each argued for their principle based on transformations.
Notice the pattern: each philosopher argues that their principle underlies all change.

ويقال إنه أخذه من التوراة لأنه جاء فى السفر الأول منها أن الله تعالى خلق جوهرا فنظر إليه بنظر الهيبة فذابت أجزاؤه فصارت ماء ثم ارتفع منه بخار كالدخان فخلق منه السمرات فظهر على وجه الماء زید فخاق منه الأرض ثم أرساها بالجبال ، وزعم انكسم ا ينس أنه الهواء وكون النار من لطافته والماء والأرض من كثافته ، وزعم أبر بليطس أنه النار وكون الأشياء عنها بالتكاتف ، وآخرون قالوا إنه الأرض وكون الأشياء عنه بالتلطيف ، وآخرون أنه البخار وكون الهواء والعار عنه بالتلطيف والماء والأرض بالتكثيف ، و عن انكساغورس أنه الخليط الذى لا نهاية له وهو أجسام غير متناهية ومنه من كل نوع أجزاء صغيرة متلا لقية : أجزاء على طبيعة الخبز وأجزاء على طبيعة اللحم فإذا اجتمع من تلك الأجزاء شيء كثير صار بحيث يحس ويرى ظن أنه حدث ، وهذا القائل بنى على هذا المذهب إنكار المزاج والاستحالة ، وقال بالكمون والظهور ، وزعم بعض هؤلاء أن ذلك الخليط كان ساكنا فى الأزل ثم إن الله تعالى حركة فكون منه هذا العالم، فزهم ديمقراطيس أن أصل العالم أجزاء صغيرة كرية الشكل قابلة للقسمة الوهمية دون القسمة الانفكاكية متحركة لذاتها حركات دائمة ، ثم اتفق فى تلك الأجزاء أن تصادفت على وجه خاص فحصل من تصادفها على ذلك الوجه هذا العالم على هذا الشكل فحدثت السموات والعناصر ، ثم حدث من الحركات السماوية امتزاجات هذه العناصر ومنها هذه المركبات ، وزعمت الثنوية أن أصل العالم هو النور والمظلمة (1) أقول: صاحب الملل والنحل نقل عن ثماليس الملطى أنه قال المبدأ الأول أبدع العنصر الذي فيه صور الموجودات والمعدومات كلها فانبحث من كل صورة موجود فى العالم على المال الذي في العنصر الأول ، فمحل الصور ومنبع الموجودات هو ذات العنصر ، وما من موجود فى العالم العقلى والعالم الحسى إلا وفي ذات المنصر صورة ومثال منه قال : ويتصور العامة أن الصور والمعلومات فى ذات المبدأ الأول لا بل هي في مبدعه وهي تعالى بوحدانيته أن يوصف بما يوصف به مبدعه ، ثم قال ومن العجب أنه نقل عنه أن المبدع الأول هو الماء منه أبدع الجواهر كلها من السماء والأرض وما بينهما ، فذكر أن من جموده تكوين الأرض ومن انخلا له تكوين الهواء، و من صفوة الهواء تكونت الدار ، ومن الدخان والأبخرة تكونت السماء فدارت حول المركز دوران المسبب على سبيه بالشوق الحاصل ، وفي الأخير قال وفي التوراة فى السفر الأول جوهر خلقه الله ثم نظر إليه إلى آخره، قال وكان ثاليس الملطى إنما تلقى مذهبه من هذه المشكاة النبوية ، قال والماء على القول الثانى شديد الشبه بالماء الذي عليه العرش وكان عرشه على الماء، وأما انكياس الملطى نقل عن مذهبه في التوحيد وخلق الأشياء ثم
The second consideration on accidents. Proposition: The people of the world differed on the temporality (حدوث) of bodies. And the possible aspects therein do not exceed four. For it is either: (1) temporally originated in essence and attributes; or (2) eternal in essence and attributes; or (3) eternal in essence, temporally originated in attributes; or (4) the opposite. As for the first: it is the statement of the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Magians. As for the second: it is the statement of Aristotle, Theophrastus, Themistius, Proclus, and among the later ones Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. They hold that the heavens are eternal in their essence and specific attributes except for movements and positions, for each of them is temporally originated and preceded by another without a first; and the elements and hyle are eternal in their individuals, and corporeality is eternal in its species, and all [other] forms are eternal in their genus, i.e., before every form there is another form without beginning. As for the third: it is the statement of the philosophers who were before Aristotle in time, such as Thales, Anaxagoras, Socrates, and the statement of all the dualists such as the Manichaeans, the Daysanites, the Marcionites, and the 'Masahaniyya' [?]. Then these are two groups. The first group: those who claimed that that matter is a body. Then Thales claimed that it is water because it is receptive of all forms. He claimed that when it solidifies it becomes earth, when it rarefies it becomes air, from the purest of air fire is formed, and from smoke the heavens are formed. It is said that he took this from the Torah, because in the first book thereof it is stated that God created a substance, He gazed at it with a gaze of awe, so its parts melted and became water, then vapor like smoke rose from it, from which He created the heavens; then foam appeared on the water's surface, from which He created the earth, then He fixed it with mountains. And Anaximenes claimed that it is air, and fire comes from its rarefaction, water and earth from its condensation. And Archelaus claimed that it is fire, and things come from it by cohesion. Others said it is earth, and things come from it by rarefaction. Others said it is vapor, and air and fire come from it by rarefaction, water and earth by condensation. And Anaxagoras said it is the infinite mixture, which is infinite bodies, and from it of every kind there are small parts that meet: parts of the nature of bread, parts of the nature of flesh. When many of those parts come together, it becomes such that it is sensed and seen, and it is thought to have come into being. This proponent built upon this doctrine the denial of mixture and transformation, and posited latency and manifestation. Some of these claimed that that mixture was quiescent in pre-eternity, then God moved it and formed this world from it. And Democritus claimed that the origin of the world is small spherical parts, divisible in estimation but not divisible by separation, moving by themselves with perpetual motions. Then these parts happened to collide in a particular manner, and from their collision in that manner this world came to be in this shape, so the heavens and elements arose, then from the celestial movements mixtures of these elements came about, and from them these composites. And the dualists claimed that the origin of the world is light and darkness. (1) I say: The author of al-Milal wa al-Nihal reported from Thales of Miletus that he said: The First Principle created the element which contains the forms of all existents and non-existents, and from every form an existent in the world emanates in accordance with the form in the first element. So the locus of forms and the source of existents is the essence of the element, and there is no existent in the intelligible world and the sensible world except that in the essence of the element there is a form and a likeness of it. He said: The common people imagine that the forms and cognitions are in the First Principle, but rather they are in His created (element). And He, exalted, is by His unity too exalted to be described by what His created is described with. Then he said: And it is amazing that it is reported from him that the First Principle is water, from which He created all the jewels/substances of heaven, earth, and what is between them. He mentioned that from its freezing comes the formation of earth, from its evaporation comes the formation of air, from the purest of air the abode [heaven?] was formed, and from smoke and vapors heaven was formed, and it revolved around the center in the manner of a waterwheel, due to the desire that occurs. And at the end he said: In the Torah, in the first book, there is a substance that God created, then He looked at it etc. He said: Thales of Miletus only received his doctrine from this prophetic niche. He said: And water according to the second statement is very similar to the water upon which the Throne was, and His Throne was upon water. And as for Anaximenes of Miletus, it is reported from him regarding his doctrine on tawhid and the creation of things, then...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This extensive passage is the beginning of the second consideration (النظر الثاني) on accidents, specifically dealing with the differing opinions on the temporal origination (حدوث) of bodies. It is a classification passage. Razi first states that the possible views are exactly four: 1) originated in essence and attributes; 2) eternal in both; 3) eternal in essence but originated in attributes; 4) the opposite (originated in essence but eternal in attributes). He then describes the first view as that of the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Magians. The second view is attributed to Aristotle and his followers (Theophrastus, Themistius, Proclus, and later al-Farabi and Ibn Sina). Their view is that the heavens are eternal in essence and in their specific attributes, except for movements and positions which are temporally originated in an infinite series. The elements and hyle are eternal as individuals, corporeality is eternal as a species, and all other forms are eternal as a genus, meaning before every form there is another form without beginning. The third view is that of the pre-Aristotelian philosophers (Thales, Anaxagoras, Socrates) and the dualists (Manichaeans, Daysanites, Marcionites, Masahaniyya). Razi then splits these into two groups. The first group (الفرقة الأولى) are those who say that the matter (المادة) is a body. Under this, he enumerates various ancient Greek philosophers and their views on the material principle: Thales (water), Anaximenes (air), Archelaus (fire), and others (earth, vapor). He also mentions Anaxagoras' theory of infinite mixture with seeds of all kinds, leading to the denial of mixture and transformation and the assertion of latency and manifestation (الكمون والظهور). Some of Anaxagoras' followers said the mixture was originally static and then God moved it. Then Democritus' atomic theory is described: small spherical parts (atoms) that are indivisible in actual separation but divisible in estimation, moving eternally, and their random collisions form the world. Finally, the dualists (الثنوية) are said to believe that the origin is light and darkness. The passage concludes with a lengthy footnote (marked (1)) which appears to be a quotation from al-Shahrastani's 'al-Milal wa al-Nihal' discussing Thales of Miletus in more detail, including a link to the Torah. This footnote comments on Thales' view that the First Principle is water and notes a similarity to the Qur'anic mention of water and the Throne. The text of the footnote is incomplete at the end.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who claimed that the fundamental matter is water?
A) Anaximenes
B) Thales
C) Archelaus
D) Socrates

Key Takeaway
Early Greek philosophers identified various material principles (water, air, fire, earth, vapor) as the basis of all existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that early philosophers sought to explain all existence through a single material principle, do you ever find yourself reducing Allah's creation to mere material causes? In what ways might you be unconsciously excluding the Creator from your daily understanding of the world?
?
Thales and others relied on observation and reason. How often do you rely solely on your own reasoning about the universe without turning to revelation? What does this lesson teach you about the limits of human philosophy?
?
The dualists mentioned here attributed creation to two principles. Do you ever act as if there are other sources of power or good besides Allah? Reflect on a specific area where you might be compromising tawheed.

Allah's Existence
advanced
5 min
Anaxagoras, Democritus, and the Dualists
Razi explains the theories of Anaxagoras, Democritus, and the dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theories of Anaxagoras (infinite mixture), Democritus (atoms), and the dualists (light and darkness) as presented by Razi.

Core Idea
Infinite mixtures, atoms, and two principles: diverse pre-Socratic and dualist cosmogonies.

Razi continues his survey of those who held that matter is a body, now turning to more complex theories. Anaxagoras posits an infinite mixture of seeds, Democritus proposes atoms in perpetual motion, and the dualists assert two fundamental principles: light and darkness.

Guided Path
1
Read Anaxagoras' theory of the infinite mixture
Anaxagoras believed in an infinite mixture of all kinds of parts, leading to the denial of genuine mixture and transformation, and positing latency and manifestation.
Note the concept of 'كمون' (latency) and 'ظهور' (manifestation).
2
Understand Democritus' atomic theory
Democritus proposed small spherical parts (atoms) that are indivisible in separation but divisible in estimation, moving perpetually and colliding to form the world.
3
Examine the dualists' view
The dualists claimed that the origin of the world is light and darkness.
This is a brief mention; no further elaboration in this passage.

The second consideration on accidents. Proposition: The people of the world differed on the temporality (حدوث) of bodies. And the possible aspects therein do not exceed four. For it is either: (1) temporally originated in essence and attributes; or (2) eternal in essence and attributes; or (3) eternal in essence, temporally originated in attributes; or (4) the opposite. As for the first: it is the statement of the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Magians. As for the second: it is the statement of Aristotle, Theophrastus, Themistius, Proclus, and among the later ones Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. They hold that the heavens are eternal in their essence and specific attributes except for movements and positions, for each of them is temporally originated and preceded by another without a first; and the elements and hyle are eternal in their individuals, and corporeality is eternal in its species, and all [other] forms are eternal in their genus, i.e., before every form there is another form without beginning. As for the third: it is the statement of the philosophers who were before Aristotle in time, such as Thales, Anaxagoras, Socrates, and the statement of all the dualists such as the Manichaeans, the Daysanites, the Marcionites, and the 'Masahaniyya' [?]. Then these are two groups. The first group: those who claimed that that matter is a body. Then Thales claimed that it is water because it is receptive of all forms. He claimed that when it solidifies it becomes earth, when it rarefies it becomes air, from the purest of air fire is formed, and from smoke the heavens are formed. It is said that he took this from the Torah, because in the first book thereof it is stated that God created a substance, He gazed at it with a gaze of awe, so its parts melted and became water, then vapor like smoke rose from it, from which He created the heavens; then foam appeared on the water's surface, from which He created the earth, then He fixed it with mountains. And Anaximenes claimed that it is air, and fire comes from its rarefaction, water and earth from its condensation. And Archelaus claimed that it is fire, and things come from it by cohesion. Others said it is earth, and things come from it by rarefaction. Others said it is vapor, and air and fire come from it by rarefaction, water and earth by condensation. And Anaxagoras said it is the infinite mixture, which is infinite bodies, and from it of every kind there are small parts that meet: parts of the nature of bread, parts of the nature of flesh. When many of those parts come together, it becomes such that it is sensed and seen, and it is thought to have come into being. This proponent built upon this doctrine the denial of mixture and transformation, and posited latency and manifestation. Some of these claimed that that mixture was quiescent in pre-eternity, then God moved it and formed this world from it. And Democritus claimed that the origin of the world is small spherical parts, divisible in estimation but not divisible by separation, moving by themselves with perpetual motions. Then these parts happened to collide in a particular manner, and from their collision in that manner this world came to be in this shape, so the heavens and elements arose, then from the celestial movements mixtures of these elements came about, and from them these composites. And the dualists claimed that the origin of the world is light and darkness. (1) I say: The author of al-Milal wa al-Nihal reported from Thales of Miletus that he said: The First Principle created the element which contains the forms of all existents and non-existents, and from every form an existent in the world emanates in accordance with the form in the first element. So the locus of forms and the source of existents is the essence of the element, and there is no existent in the intelligible world and the sensible world except that in the essence of the element there is a form and a likeness of it. He said: The common people imagine that the forms and cognitions are in the First Principle, but rather they are in His created (element). And He, exalted, is by His unity too exalted to be described by what His created is described with. Then he said: And it is amazing that it is reported from him that the First Principle is water, from which He created all the jewels/substances of heaven, earth, and what is between them. He mentioned that from its freezing comes the formation of earth, from its evaporation comes the formation of air, from the purest of air the abode [heaven?] was formed, and from smoke and vapors heaven was formed, and it revolved around the center in the manner of a waterwheel, due to the desire that occurs. And at the end he said: In the Torah, in the first book, there is a substance that God created, then He looked at it etc. He said: Thales of Miletus only received his doctrine from this prophetic niche. He said: And water according to the second statement is very similar to the water upon which the Throne was, and His Throne was upon water. Commentary
This extensive passage is the beginning of the second consideration (النظر الثاني) on accidents, specifically dealing with the differing opinions on the temporal origination (حدوث) of bodies. It is a classification passage. Razi first states that the possible views are exactly four: 1) originated in essence and attributes; 2) eternal in both; 3) eternal in essence but originated in attributes; 4) the opposite (originated in essence but eternal in attributes). He then describes the first view as that of the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Magians. The second view is attributed to Aristotle and his followers (Theophrastus, Themistius, Proclus, and later al-Farabi and Ibn Sina). Their view is that the heavens are eternal in essence and in their specific attributes, except for movements and positions which are temporally originated in an infinite series. The elements and hyle are eternal as individuals, corporeality is eternal as a species, and all other forms are eternal as a genus, meaning before every form there is another form without beginning. The third view is that of the pre-Aristotelian philosophers (Thales, Anaxagoras, Socrates) and the dualists (Manichaeans, Daysanites, Marcionites, Masahaniyya). Razi then splits these into two groups. The first group (الفرقة الأولى) are those who say that the matter (المادة) is a body. Under this, he enumerates various ancient Greek philosophers and their views on the material principle: Thales (water), Anaximenes (air), Archelaus (fire), and others (earth, vapor). He also mentions Anaxagoras' theory of infinite mixture with seeds of all kinds, leading to the denial of mixture and transformation and the assertion of latency and manifestation (الكمون والظهور). Some of Anaxagoras' followers said the mixture was originally static and then God moved it. Then Democritus' atomic theory is described: small spherical parts (atoms) that are indivisible in actual separation but divisible in estimation, moving eternally, and their random collisions form the world. Finally, the dualists (الثنوية) are said to believe that the origin is light and darkness. The passage concludes with a lengthy footnote (marked (1)) which appears to be a quotation from al-Shahrastani's 'al-Milal wa al-Nihal' discussing Thales of Miletus in more detail, including a link to the Torah. This footnote comments on Thales' view that the First Principle is water and notes a similarity to the Qur'anic mention of water and the Throne. The text of the footnote is incomplete at the end.

D) They are water

Key Takeaway
Anaxagoras, Democritus, and the dualists each offered unique materialistic or dualistic explanations for the origin of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider Democritus' claim that the world came from random collisions of atoms with no purpose, do you ever catch yourself living as though your own life, relationships, or struggles are just random accidents? How does this lesson challenge you to see purpose and design in your own story?
?
Anaxagoras said that apparent coming-into-being is just aggregation of pre-existing parts, denying true creation. Reflect on a moment when you attributed something to 'just happening' rather than acknowledging Allah's direct act of creation. How can you train your heart to see His creative hand in every detail of your day?
?
The dualists claimed two eternal principles: light and darkness. Where in your life do you act as if there are two independent powers—good and evil—rather than affirming that Allah alone is the Creator of all, including darkness? What would it look like to submit even the 'dark' aspects of your life to His oneness?

Footnote: Thales, the Torah, and the Water of the Throne
Razi (quoting al-Shahrastani) explores Thales' water doctrine and its scriptural parallels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the footnote's discussion of Thales' water doctrine and its purported scriptural origins from the Torah and the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Thales' water principle may be linked to the Torah's creation account and the Qur'anic 'water upon which the Throne was'.

At the end of the passage, Razi adds a lengthy footnote quoting al-Shahrastani's 'al-Milal wa al-Nihal'. This footnote delves deeper into Thales of Miletus, presenting a version of his doctrine that includes the idea that the First Principle created an element containing all forms. It also connects Thales' view to the Torah and to the Qur'anic verse about the Throne being upon water.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote passage
The footnote begins with 'أقول: صاحب الملل والنحل نقل عن ثماليس الملطى' and discusses Thales' doctrine at length.
This is a quotation from al-Shahrastani.
2
Understand the claim about the Torah
The footnote reports that Thales took his water theory from the Torah's account of God creating a substance that became water.
3
Note the connection to the Qur'an
The footnote ends by noting the similarity to the Qur'anic 'water upon which the Throne was' and saying Thales' doctrine came from a 'prophetic niche'.
This shows an attempt to harmonize Greek philosophy with revealed scripture.

The second consideration on accidents. Proposition: The people of the world differed on the temporality (حدوث) of bodies. And the possible aspects therein do not exceed four. For it is either: (1) temporally originated in essence and attributes; or (2) eternal in essence and attributes; or (3) eternal in essence, temporally originated in attributes; or (4) the opposite. As for the first: it is the statement of the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Magians. As for the second: it is the statement of Aristotle, Theophrastus, Themistius, Proclus, and among the later ones Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. They hold that the heavens are eternal in their essence and specific attributes except for movements and positions, for each of them is temporally originated and preceded by another without a first; and the elements and hyle are eternal in their individuals, and corporeality is eternal in its species, and all [other] forms are eternal in their genus, i.e., before every form there is another form without beginning. As for the third: it is the statement of the philosophers who were before Aristotle in time, such as Thales, Anaxagoras, Socrates, and the statement of all the dualists such as the Manichaeans, the Daysanites, the Marcionites, and the 'Masahaniyya' [?]. Then these are two groups. The first group: those who claimed that that matter is a body. Then Thales claimed that it is water because it is receptive of all forms. He claimed that when it solidifies it becomes earth, when it rarefies it becomes air, from the purest of air fire is formed, and from smoke the heavens are formed. It is said that he took this from the Torah, because in the first book thereof it is stated that God created a substance, He gazed at it with a gaze of awe, so its parts melted and became water, then vapor like smoke rose from it, from which He created the heavens; then foam appeared on the water's surface, from which He created the earth, then He fixed it with mountains. And Anaximenes claimed that it is air, and fire comes from its rarefaction, water and earth from its condensation. And Archelaus claimed that it is fire, and things come from it by cohesion. Others said it is earth, and things come from it by rarefaction. Others said it is vapor, and air and fire come from it by rarefaction, water and earth by condensation. And Anaxagoras said it is the infinite mixture, which is infinite bodies, and from it of every kind there are small parts that meet: parts of the nature of bread, parts of the nature of flesh. When many of those parts come together, it becomes such that it is sensed and seen, and it is thought to have come into being. This proponent built upon this doctrine the denial of mixture and transformation, and posited latency and manifestation. Some of these claimed that that mixture was quiescent in pre-eternity, then God moved it and formed this world from it. And Democritus claimed that the origin of the world is small spherical parts, divisible in estimation but not divisible by separation, moving by themselves with perpetual motions. Then these parts happened to collide in a particular manner, and from their collision in that manner this world came to be in this shape, so the heavens and elements arose, then from the celestial movements mixtures of these elements came about, and from them these composites. And the dualists claimed that the origin of the world is light and darkness. (1) I say: The author of al-Milal wa al-Nihal reported from Thales of Miletus that he said: The First Principle created the element which contains the forms of all existents and non-existents, and from every form an existent in the world emanates in accordance with the form in the first element. So the locus of forms and the source of existents is the essence of the element, and there is no existent in the intelligible world and the sensible world except that in the essence of the element there is a form and a likeness of it. He said: The common people imagine that the forms and cognitions are in the First Principle, but rather they are in His created (element). And He, exalted, is by His unity too exalted to be described by what His created is described with. Then he said: And it is amazing that it is reported from him that the First Principle is water, from which He created all the jewels/substances of heaven, earth, and what is between them. He mentioned that from its freezing comes the formation of earth, from its evaporation comes the formation of air, from the purest of air the abode [heaven?] was formed, and from smoke and vapors heaven was formed, and it revolved around the center in the manner of a waterwheel, due to the desire that occurs. And at the end he said: In the Torah, in the first book, there is a substance that God created, then He looked at it etc. He said: Thales of Miletus only received his doctrine from this prophetic niche. He said: And water according to the second statement is very similar to the water upon which the Throne was, and His Throne was upon water. And as for Anaximenes of Miletus, it is reported from him regarding his doctrine on tawhid and the creation of things, then...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This extensive passage is the beginning of the second consideration (النظر الثاني) on accidents, specifically dealing with the differing opinions on the temporal origination (حدوث) of bodies. It is a classification passage. Razi first states that the possible views are exactly four: 1) originated in essence and attributes; 2) eternal in both; 3) eternal in essence but originated in attributes; 4) the opposite (originated in essence but eternal in attributes). He then describes the first view as that of the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Magians. The second view is attributed to Aristotle and his followers (Theophrastus, Themistius, Proclus, and later al-Farabi and Ibn Sina). Their view is that the heavens are eternal in essence and in their specific attributes, except for movements and positions which are temporally originated in an infinite series. The elements and hyle are eternal as individuals, corporeality is eternal as a species, and all other forms are eternal as a genus, meaning before every form there is another form without beginning. The third view is that of the pre-Aristotelian philosophers (Thales, Anaxagoras, Socrates) and the dualists (Manichaeans, Daysanites, Marcionites, Masahaniyya). Razi then splits these into two groups. The first group (الفرقة الأولى) are those who say that the matter (المادة) is a body. Under this, he enumerates various ancient Greek philosophers and their views on the material principle: Thales (water), Anaximenes (air), Archelaus (fire), and others (earth, vapor). He also mentions Anaxagoras' theory of infinite mixture with seeds of all kinds, leading to the denial of mixture and transformation and the assertion of latency and manifestation (الكمون والظهور). Some of Anaxagoras' followers said the mixture was originally static and then God moved it. Then Democritus' atomic theory is described: small spherical parts (atoms) that are indivisible in actual separation but divisible in estimation, moving eternally, and their random collisions form the world. Finally, the dualists (الثنوية) are said to believe that the origin is light and darkness. The passage concludes with a lengthy footnote (marked (1)) which appears to be a quotation from al-Shahrastani's 'al-Milal wa al-Nihal' discussing Thales of Miletus in more detail, including a link to the Torah. This footnote comments on Thales' view that the First Principle is water and notes a similarity to the Qur'anic mention of water and the Throne. The text of the footnote is incomplete at the end.

?
The common people mistakenly placed forms in the First Principle itself. In your own life, are there concepts about God that you hold onto merely because they are common, without checking whether they truly reflect His transcendence? How can you guard against such assumptions?

The Jermanian Doctrine of Five Eternal Principles
Exposition of Jermanian cosmology and theodicy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jermanian doctrine of five eternal principles and how they explain the world's creation, its timing, and the presence of evil.

Core Idea
The Jermanians resolve the debate on creation vs. eternity by linking the world's origin to the soul's attachment to matter.

The Jermanians were a group within Islamic thought who proposed a unique cosmology involving five eternal principles. This passage from Imam al-Razi presents their view on the Creator, soul, matter, time, and void, and how this framework answers questions about why the world was created at a particular time and why evil exists. We will examine their position and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the Jermanian doctrine. Pay attention to the five eternal principles and the flow of the argument.
The passage uses technical terms like الهيولى (matter) and الدهر (time).
2
Identify the five principles
List the five eternal things: the Creator, the soul, matter, time, and void. Note how each is defined or described in the passage.
These are the foundations of Jermanian cosmology.
3
Understand the soul's role
Focus on the description of the soul: it is ignorant initially, loves matter, and seeks sensory pleasures. The Creator knows this and creates the world accordingly.
The soul's attachment to matter triggers creation.
4
See the solution to the debate
Notice how the Jermanians address the classic questions: why creation at a specific time? and why evil? They attribute the timing to the soul's attachment, and evil to the limitations of matter.
This harmonizes divine wisdom with the world's imperfections.
5
Reflect on theodicy
Consider the Jermanian explanation of evil: it is inevitable because of composition, but the Creator arranges things in the best possible way. Think about how this compares to other theodicies.
The passage ends with the claim that their approach removes previous doubts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثانية : الذين قالوا : أصل العالم ليس بجسم وهم فريقان الفرقة الأولى الجرمانية وهم الذين ثبتوا القدماء الخمسة البارى تعالى والنفس والهيولى والدهر والخارة ، فقالوا البارى تعالى باب العلم والحكمة لا يعرض هو ولا غفلة وفيض عنه المقل كفيض النور عن القرص ، وهو تعالى يعرف الأشياء معرفة تامة ، وأما النفس فإنه يفيض عنها الحياة فيض النور عن القرص لكنها جاهلة لا تعلم إلا شيئا ما لم تمارسها . وكان البارى تعالى عالما بأن النفس تميل إلى التعلق بالهيولى وتعشقها وتطلب اللذة الحسية وتكره مفارقة الأجسام وتنسى نفسها ، فلما كان من شأن البارى تعالى الحكمة التامة أوجد الهيولى بعد تعلق النفس بها فركبها ضروبا من التراكيب مثل السموات والعناصر وركب أجسام الحيوانات على الوجه الأكمل والذي فيها من الفساد فذلك لأنه لا يمكن إلا ذلك ، ثم إنه تعالى أفاض على النفس عقلا وإدراكا وصار ذلك سبيلا لتذكرها عالمها وسببا لعلمها بأنها مادامت في عالم الهيولى لا تنفك من الآلام وإذا عرفت النفس ذلك وعرفت أن لها في عالمها اللذات الخالية عن الآلام اشتاقت إلى ذلك العالم وعرجت عليه بعد المفارقة وبقيت هناك إلى أبد الآباد في نهاية البهجة والسعادة، قالوا وبهذا الطريق زالت الشبهات الدائرة بين القائلين بالقدم والحدوث . فإن أصحاب القدم قالوا لو كان العالم محدثا فلم أحدثه تعالى في هذا الوقت المعين وما أحدثه قبل ذلك ولا بعده ، وإن كان خالق العالم حكيما فلم ولا الدنيا من الآفات . وأصحاب الحدوث قالوا : لو كان العالم قديما لكان غنيا عن الفاعل وهذا باطل قطعا لما نرى أن آثار الحكمة ظاهرة في العالم وتحير الفريقان في ذلك وأما على هذا الطريق فالإشكالات زائلة لأنا لما اعترفنا بالصانع الحكيم لاجرم قلنا بحدوث العالم فإذا قيل ولم أحدث العالم في هذا الوقت ؟ قلنا لأن النفس لما تعلقت بالهيولى في ذلك الوقت وعلم البارى تعالى أن ذلك الفساد إلا أنه بعد وقوع المحذور صرفه إلى الوجه الأكمل بحسب الإمكان ، وأما الشرور الباقية فكلها فإنما بقيت لأنه لا يمكن تحديد هذا التركيب عنها .
The second group: those who said: the principle of the world is not a body, and they are two groups. The first group: the Jermanians, who affirmed the five eternal things: the Creator (al-Bāriʾ), the soul (al-Nafs), matter (al-Hayūlā), time (al-Dahr), and void (al-Khālaʾ). They said: the Creator, possessing knowledge and wisdom, does not befall Him heedlessness or neglect, and the intellect flows from Him like the flow of light from the disc. He knows things with complete knowledge. As for the soul, life flows from it like the flow of light from the disc, but it is ignorant and knows nothing unless it practices. The Creator knew that the soul inclines to attachment to matter, loves it, seeks sensory pleasure, dislikes parting from bodies, and forgets itself. Since it was the case that the Creator possesses perfect wisdom, He brought matter into existence after the soul's attachment to it, and He composed it into various compositions like the heavens and the elements, and composed animal bodies in the most perfect manner. What is in them of corruption is because it cannot be otherwise. Then He poured upon the soul intellect and perception, and that became a way for it to remember its world and a cause for it to know that as long as it is in the world of matter, it will not be free from pains. When the soul knows that and knows that it has pleasures free from pain in its own world, it yearns for that world and ascends to it after separation, remaining there forever in utmost joy and happiness. They said: by this way, the doubts that circulate between those who affirm eternity and those who affirm temporal origination are removed. For the partisans of eternity said: if the world were originated, why did He originate it at this particular time and not before or after? And if the Creator of the world is wise, why is the world filled with afflictions? The partisans of temporal origination said: if the world were eternal, it would be independent of an agent, and this is clearly false because we see that manifestations of wisdom are apparent in the world. The two groups were perplexed. But on this way, the problems are removed: since we have affirmed the wise Maker, we must of necessity affirm the world's temporal origination. If it is said: why did He originate the world at this time? We say: because the soul attached to matter at that time, and the Creator knew that corruption [would result], yet after the occurrence of the undesirable, He redirected it to the most perfect manner according to possibility. As for the remaining evils, they all remain only because it is impossible to eliminate this composition entirely.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of the Jermanians (a subgroup of the second group who hold that the principle of the world is not a body). It begins by listing their five eternal principles: the Creator (al-Bāriʾ), the soul (al-Nafs), matter (al-Hayūlā), time (al-Dahr), and void (al-Khālaʾ). The description then explains the nature of the Creator: He is all-knowing and wise, free from heedlessness, and intellect emanates from Him. The soul, by contrast, is initially ignorant and gains knowledge only through practice. The Creator knows that the soul naturally inclines to attach to matter and seek sensual pleasures. In His perfect wisdom, after the soul's attachment to matter, He creates the world in the best possible way, including the heavens, elements, and animal bodies. The inevitable corruption in the world is due to the limitations of matter. Then He grants the soul intellect, enabling it to remember its higher origin and realize that attachment to matter brings pain. This knowledge makes the soul yearn for its original world and ascend there after death. This, they claim, resolves the classic debate between those who argue for the world's eternity (who ask why the world was created at a specific time if it is originated) and those who argue for its temporal origination (who say an eternal world would have no need of a Creator). The Jermanian solution: the world is created at the time of the soul's attachment to matter; the specific time is determined by that attachment; evils remain because complete removal of composition is impossible, and what can be fixed is done so optimally. The passage thus presents a theodicy and a harmonization of divine wisdom with the existence of evil.

Key Terms

البارى
النفس
الهيولى
الدهر
الخارة
الحدوث
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jermanians were atheists or denied a Creator.
Correction: The Jermanians explicitly affirm a Creator (al-Bāriʾ) as one of the five eternal principles and describe Him as all-knowing and wise.

Checkpoint
According to the Jermanians, what are the five eternal things?
A) Creator, soul, matter, time, void
B) Creator, intellect, soul, matter, time
C) Creator, soul, matter, form, void
D) Creator, soul, heaven, earth, void

Key Takeaway
The Jermanians posit five eternal principles and explain the world's creation and evil through the soul's attachment to matter and the limitations of composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Jermanians say that the soul yearns to ascend from the world of matter after realizing the pain it causes. What specific attachment to this material life do you find hardest to let go of, and why do you think you cling to it despite knowing it brings pain?
?
They claim that the wise Creator arranged the world in the most perfect manner possible given the soul's attachment. If you truly believed that every difficulty you face is part of that optimal arrangement, how would you respond to your current suffering instead of complaining or despairing?
?
The passage teaches that the soul forgets itself when immersed in bodies. In what area of your life have you forgotten your true origin—your purpose beyond this world—and what step will you take today to begin remembering?

Dismissing a Contradictory View
Razi dismisses the fourth division and mentions Galen's skepticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that the world is eternal in attributes but temporal in essence is considered irrational, and how Razi uses this to close a classification.

Core Idea
A logically incoherent view is dismissed as irrational; true skepticism suspends judgment.

In this brief passage, Razi completes his classification of possible views about the world. After examining coherent positions, he dismisses the contradictory fourth division as something no rational person would propose. He then notes that Galen, the famous physician, adopted a stance of suspension of judgment rather than committing to any view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: Razi states the view and immediately dismisses it.
2
Understand the classification context
This is the fourth division in a four-part classification of views about the world's eternity or origination. The first three divisions were discussed previously; this one is presented as impossible.
The phrase 'القسم الرابع' signals the end of the classification.
3
Identify why it is dismissed
The view combines 'qadīm' (eternal) and 'muḥdath' (temporal) in a contradictory way. Razi says no rational person (عاقل) would say this.
The contradiction lies in attributing eternal attributes to a temporally originated essence.
4
Relate to Galen's position
Galen is mentioned as suspending judgment (متوقف) on everything. This shows that even a skeptic did not endorse this contradictory view; he simply withheld judgment.
Galen's view is separate from the classification; it illustrates a stance of skepticism.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why rational coherence is important in theological arguments. The dismissal is based on logical impossibility, not on authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الرابع : وهو أن يقال العالم قديم الصفات محدث الذات فذلك مما لا يقوله عاقل ، وأما جالينوس فقد كان متوقفا فى الكل .
As for the fourth division, that it is said the world is eternal in attributes and temporal in essence, that is something no rational person would say. As for Galen, he was suspending judgment about everything.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This very short passage concludes a classification of views about the world. After discussing views that the world is eternal (qadīm) or originated (muḥdath) in various ways, Razi states that the fourth possibility—that the world is eternal in its attributes but temporally originated in its essence—is absurd; no rational person (ʿāqil) would propose it. He then notes that Galen (Jālīnūs) was a skeptic (mutawaqqif) suspending judgment entirely. The passage serves to close the discussion of the second group (those who say the principle of the world is not a body) by dismissing a logically incoherent position.

Key Terms
القسم الرابع
قديم الصفات
محدث الذات
جالينوس
متوقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the fourth division is a defensible position held by some rational thinkers.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that no rational person (عاقل) would say such a thing, implying it is beyond the pale of rational discourse.

Checkpoint
What was Galen's position regarding the world's eternity or origination?
A) He believed the world is eternal.
B) He believed the world is temporal in essence but eternal in attributes.
C) He suspended judgment.
D) He believed the world is originated.

Key Takeaway
The claim that the world is eternal in attributes but temporal in essence is rationally absurd; Galen's skepticism is not a commitment to any view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, upon honest reflection, contradicts itself? What is holding you back from admitting its absurdity, and how might letting it go change your relationship with the truth?
?
Galen suspended judgment on everything. Is there an area of your faith where you are avoiding a decision—perhaps about a command of Allah or a matter of creed—out of comfort rather than conviction? What step will you take this week to move from suspension to certainty?
?
Razi dismisses the fourth division as something no rational person would say. When you hear an argument that seems irrational, do you dismiss it quickly, or do you examine it to see if you might be misunderstanding? Where in your life might you be too hasty in labeling something as absurd, and how can you cultivate humility in seeking truth?

Proof that Bodies Cannot Be Eternal: The Impossibility of Eternal Motion and Rest
Proof that bodies cannot be eternal by showing eternal motion and rest are impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why theologians argue that bodies cannot be eternal, based on the impossibility of eternal motion or rest.

Core Idea
Eternal motion or rest is impossible, so bodies cannot be eternal.

This lesson presents a key argument from Imam al-Razi against the eternity of bodies. The argument proceeds by dividing the possible states of an eternal body—motion or rest—and demonstrates that both lead to contradictions. Understanding this proof is essential for grasping the Islamic theological view that the universe is created.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dichotomy
Recognize that an eternal body must be either moving or stationary at pre-eternity.
2
Examine the two reasons against eternal motion
Follow the two arguments: one based on the essence of motion, the other on the need for a free agent. Both show eternal motion is contradictory.
3
Examine the two reasons against eternal rest
See why eternal rest is impossible: either motion becomes impossible permanently or rest itself leads to a regress.
4
Consider the broader implication
Realize that if both states fail, bodies cannot be eternal, supporting the createdness of the universe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن الأجسام لو كانت أزلية لكانت في الأزل إما متحركة أو ساكنة والقسمان باطلان فالقول بأزليتها باطل .
بيان الحصر : أن الجسم إن كان مستقراً في مكان واحد أكثر من زمان واحد فهو الساكن وإن لم يكن كذلك كان متحركا ، وإنما قلنا إنه لا يجوز أن يكون متحركا لوجهين .
الأول : أن ماهية الحركة حصول أمر بعد فناء غيره فماهيتها تقتضى المسبوقية بالغير ، والأزلية ماهيتها تقتضى عدم المسبوقية بالغير والجمع بينهما متناقض والثانى : وهو أن كل واحد من الحركات محدث فهو مفتقر إلى موجد وكل ما كان كل واحد منه مفتقرا إلى الموجد فكل الحركات تفتقر إلى موجد مختار فكل ما كان فعلا للفاعل المختار فلا بد له من أول ، فلكل الحركات أول وهو المطلوب .
وإنما قلنا إنها لا يجوز أن تكون ساكنة لوجين.
الأول : أنها لو كانت ساكنة لكان إما أن يصح ليها الحركة أولا يصح والأول محال ، لأن صحة الحركة عليها متوقف على صحة وجود الحركة في نفسها ، وقد دللنا أن وجود الحركة الأزلية محال ، فثبت أن لا تصح الحركة عليها ، فذلك الإمتناع إن كان لازماً للماهية وجب أن لا تزول البتة فوجب أن لا تصح الحركة على الأجسام فيما لا يزال هذا خلف، وإن لم يكن من لوازم الماهية أمكن زوالها ويكون الحركة عليه جائزة وقد أبطلناه. الثاني : أن السكون أمر ثبوتى على ما دللنا عليه فتقول لو كان ذلك السكون قديما لامتنع زواله لكنه يزول فليس بقديم ، بيان الملازمة أن القديم إن كان واجبا لذاته يمتنع عدمه وإن لم يكن واجبا لذاته افتقر إلى مؤثر فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى الواجب لذاته قطعاً للتسلسل على ما سيأتى فذاك الواجب إما أن يكون مختاراً أو موجباً ، لا يجوز أن يكون مختاراً لأن فعل المختار محدث لاستحالة إيجاد الموجد والقديم ليس بمحدث فتعين أن يكون موجباً ، فإن لم يترتب تأثيره فيه على شرط لزم من وجوب ذلك المؤثر وجوب الأثر ، وإن توقف وجوب العلة على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان ممكنا عاد التقسيم فى الحاجة ، وإن كان واجبا لامتناع زوال ذلك القديم، وإما أنه يمكن عدم السكون فهو مشاهد في الفلكيات والعنصريات ولا جسم هذين عند الخصم ، ومن أراد تعميم الدلالة فلا بد له من بيان تماثل الأجسام ، ولما ثبت فساد كون الجسم متحركا أو ساكنا فى الأزل صار الجسم مستحيلا أن يكون أزليا .
We [argue] that if bodies were eternal, they would be in pre-eternity either moving or stationary, and both divisions are false, so the claim of their eternity is false. Explanation of the dichotomy: The body, if it is settled in one place for more than one moment, it is stationary; and if not, it is moving. We said that it cannot be moving for two reasons. First: The essence (mahiyya) of motion is the occurrence of a thing after the passing of another, so its essence requires being preceded by another, and the essence of eternity requires the absence of being preceded by another, and combining them is contradictory. Second: Each individual motion is temporally originated (muhdath), so it is in need of a cause; and if each individual is in need of a cause, then all motions are in need of a free acting cause (mujid mukhtar); and whatever is the act of a free agent must have a first; so all motions have a first, and that is what is sought. And we said that it cannot be stationary for two reasons. First: If it were stationary, either motion is possible for it or not; the first is impossible because the possibility of motion for it depends on the possibility of the existence of motion in itself, and we have proven that the existence of eternal motion is impossible. So it is established that motion is not possible for it. If that impossibility is inseparable from the essence, then it must never disappear, so it must be that motion is not possible for bodies in the future — this is a contradiction [since bodies move]. If it is not among the inseparables, then its removal is possible and motion would be possible for it, and we have already disproven that [i.e., we proved motion impossible]. Second: Rest (sukun) is a positive thing (amr thubuti) as we have argued. So we say: If that rest were eternal, its removal would be impossible, but it does remove, so it is not eternal. Explanation of the entailment: If the eternal is necessary by itself, its non-existence is impossible; if it is not necessary by itself, it requires an effecter; so one must end at the necessary by itself to stop regress, as will come. That necessary is either free (mukhtar) or necessitating (mujib). It cannot be free because the act of a free agent is temporally originated (muhdath) due to the impossibility of [the free agent] bringing about an eternal [act]; and the eternal is not temporally originated; so it is determined that it is necessitating. If its effect does not depend on a condition, then the necessity of the effect follows from the necessity of the cause; if the necessity of the cause depends on a condition, then if that condition is possible, the division recurs regarding need; if it is necessary, then the non-removal of that eternal [rest] is impossible. But that rest's non-existence is possible — it is observed in the celestial and elemental bodies — and there is no body of these two [types] according to the opponent. And whoever wants to generalize the proof must demonstrate the similarity of bodies. And since it has been established that it is impossible for a body to be moving or stationary in pre-eternity, the body becomes impossible to be eternal.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the eternity of bodies. It presents a dichotomy: an eternal body must be either moving or stationary in pre-eternity. Both are shown impossible. For motion: two reasons. First, motion's essence requires being preceded by another (since it is the occurrence of a thing after another), while eternity requires no precedence — contradiction. Second, each motion is originated, so requires a cause; all motions together require a free agent cause, whose act must have a first, so motion cannot be beginningless. For rest: two reasons. First, if a body were eternally at rest, either motion is possible for it or not. If motion is possible, then since motion's existence is impossible eternally (as proven), its possibility is also impossible; if motion is impossible for the essence, then it would be permanently impossible, contradicting observed motion. Second, rest is a positive attribute; if it were eternal, it would be necessary and impossible to remove, but we observe its removal (bodies start moving), so it is not eternal. The argument then considers the nature of the eternal: if necessary by itself, it cannot cease; if contingent, it requires an effecter, leading to a necessary being that is either free or necessitating. Since the free agent's act must be originated, the eternal rest cannot be from a free agent, so it would be from a necessitating cause, which would entail the rest's eternity if unconditional, or if conditional, the condition must be necessary, making rest unremovable — again contradicting observation. Thus bodies cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
أزلية (azaliyya)
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukun)
ماهية (mahiyya)
محدث (muhdath)
موجد (mujid)
مختار (mukhtar)
موجب (mujib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal motion is possible if God wills it.
Correction: The argument shows that motion's essence requires being preceded by another, which contradicts eternity's lack of precedence; also, a free agent's act must have a first, so motion cannot be beginningless.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason against eternal motion, why is a body being eternally moving impossible?
A) Because motion requires a mover, which cannot be eternal.
B) Because motion's essence requires being preceded by another, while eternity requires no precedence.
C) Because all motions are accidental and cannot be eternal.
D) Because motion implies change, which is impossible for eternal things.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of eternal motion and eternal rest demonstrates that bodies cannot be eternal, implying the universe is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof argues that every motion is temporally originated and dependent on a Creator. When you move your hand or speak a word, do you consciously recognize that Allah is creating that action? What daily habit could you adopt to remind yourself of this dependence?
?
Eternal rest is shown to be impossible because it would be unremovable. Are there areas in your life where you have assumed that a certain comfort or state of peace will last forever? How does this proof challenge your attachment to the stability of worldly life?
?
If bodies cannot be eternal, then your own body is temporal and will perish. How does this affect your priorities today? Write down one possession or ambition you are holding onto as if it will last forever, and commit to releasing it in your heart before Allah.
