Objection against the Proof of the World's Temporal Origination
An objection challenging the proof of the world's temporal origination with two arguments, the first complete, the second incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of a logical objection to the proof that the world had a temporal beginning, focusing on the first argument that the possibility of the world cannot have a first moment.

Core Idea
Possibility is essential; it cannot change from impossible to possible.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection from those who argued that the world's possibility is eternal. The objector claims that if the possibility of the world had a beginning, it would imply an absurd change in the nature of possibility itself. We will examine the first part of this objection in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the objector's claim that the proof is contradictory.
The passage is incomplete; the second argument cuts off.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
The objection is divided into two arguments: the first is complete, the second is incomplete. Identify the markers 'الأول' and 'وثانيهما'.
3
Analyze the first argument's logic
The first argument argues that the possibility of the world cannot have a first moment. If it did, before that moment the world would be impossible by itself, implying a change from impossible to possible. The objector says this is absurd because it would allow change in the nature of God and destroy certainty.
Focus on the phrase 'وإذا جوزتم ذلك لجوزوا...' to see the reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the objector thinks allowing change in possibility would 'remove certainty from rational propositions.' This is a key point in the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل الدعوى متناقضة أو جرين : الأول : أن إمكان وجود العالم ليس الأول وإلا فقد كان قبل ذلك محالا لذاته أي انقلب مسكنا لكن ذلك باطل ؛ لأن الإمكان المسكن ضرورى فيكون العالم مثل ذلك الوقت ممتنع الاتصاف بالوجود لذاته بالإمكان ثم صار واجب الاتصاف به لذاته ، وإذا جوزتم ذلك لجوزوا أنه كان ممتنع الاتصال بالوجود لذاته ثم صار واجب الاتصاف به لذاته ، ويلزمكم فى الصانع وهذا محال ، ولأنه لو جاز أن ينقلب الممتنع لداته ممكنا اذاته جاز ذلك في شريك الإله والجمع بين الصدين وهو يرفع الأمان من القضايا المقلية ، وإذا ثبت أنه لا أول لإمكان وجود العالم كان القول بأنه ممتنع الوجود في الأزل منافيا له فكان باطلا . وثانيهما : أنكم إما أن تفسروا المحدث بأنه الذي يكون مسبوقا بعدم نفسه أو بأنه الذى يكون بوجود
It was said: The claim is contradictory or [???]. First: That the possibility of the world's existence has no first, otherwise it would have been before that impossible by itself, i.e., it changed to settled, but that is false; because the possibility [??] is necessary, so the world at that time is impossible to be described by existence by itself with possibility, then it became necessary to be described by it by itself. And if you allow that, you would allow that it was impossible to be described by existence by itself then became necessary to be described by it by itself, and that would be required of you regarding the Maker, and that is impossible. And because if it were permissible for the impossible by itself to change to possible by itself, it would be permissible regarding a partner of God and the combination of the two opposites, and that removes certainty from rational propositions. And if it is established that there is no first for the possibility of the world's existence, then the statement that it is impossible in pre-eternity contradicts that and is false. Second: That you either interpret the temporally originated (muhdath) as that which is preceded by its own non-existence, or as that which is [cut off] with existence
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the proof that the world had a temporal beginning. The objector begins by claiming the proof is contradictory. The first argument: The possibility of the world's existence has no first (i.e., it is eternal). If it had a first, then before that first the world would be impossible by itself, and then it would have changed from impossible to possible or necessary. The objector argues this is absurd because it would imply that something impossible by nature changes, which would also apply to God (impossible) and to a partner of God and combining opposites, destroying certainty. Therefore, the possibility of the world must be eternal, so the claim that the world is impossible in pre-eternity is false. The second argument (incomplete) begins by discussing the definition of 'temporally originated' (muhdath) as either preceded by its own non-existence or as something with existence. The text cuts off, so the full objection and any answer are missing.

Key Terms

امتناع (imtina')
محدث (muhdath)
قديم (qadim)
واجب (wajib)
ممكن (mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility can change over time, e.g., something impossible can become possible.
Correction: The objector argues that essential possibility (imkan dhati) is immutable; if something is impossible by itself, it cannot become possible, as that would entail a change in its essence and undermine all rational certainty.

C) That the world is eternal because rest is impossible.
D) That the maker must be free and not necessitating.

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that the possibility of the world cannot have a first moment, as that would imply a change in the nature of possibility itself, which is rationally untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objection warns that allowing change in essential natures would lead to absurdities like a partner for God. Are there any beliefs or practices in your life that implicitly treat something created as having an independent essential power? Interpreting 'Preceded by Non-Existence' in Defining Temporality
Analyzes five interpretations of 'preceded by non-existence'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different senses of 'preceded by non-existence' and why the temporal sense leads to the eternity of time.

Core Idea
Not every 'precedence' is temporal; temporal precedence would make time eternal.

In theological debates about the temporality of the world, the phrase 'preceded by non-existence' is often used. But does it always mean temporal precedence? The author examines five possible interpretations, showing that only temporal precedence leads to a problematic consequence: the eternity of time itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its structure. Then read the translation to understand the argument.
Notice how the author systematically lists and evaluates each type of precedence.
2
Identify the five types of precedence
List the five types mentioned: causality, nobility, place, nature, and time. Understand each one.
The author dismisses the first three as invalid by consensus.
3
Analyze the argument for temporal precedence
Focus on the argument that temporal precedence implies the eternity of time. Why does this follow?
The concept of temporal precedence requires time to exist, so time must be first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان الأول فإما أن تريدوا به أن العدم سابق عليه بالعلية أو بالشرف أو بالمكان والكل باطل بالاتفاق، أو تريدوا بأن العدم سابق عليه بالطبع لأن الممكن المستحق العدم من ذاته والوجود من غيره وما بالذات أسبق ما بالغير أو تريدوا به السبق بالزمان فهذا يوجب قدم الزمان، لأنه إذا لم يكن مفهوم ذلك السبق أول وكان ذلك المفهوم مقتضى تحقق الزمان ازم أن لا يكون الزمان أول، ثم يلزم من قدم الزمان قدم الحركة والجسم على ما هو معلوم فالقول على هذا الوجه يوجب قدم الزمان، وأما إن فسرتم الحدوث بكونه مسبوقاً بوجود الله تعالى فإن أردتم السبق بالعلية أو بالطبع أو بالشرف فالكل مسلم والسبق بالمكان باطل بالاتفاق، وأما بالزمان فإنه يوجب قدم الزمان على ما تقدم، وإن أردتم بالحدوث معنى ثالثا فليذكروه اتكلم عليه .
If it is the first, then either you mean that non-existence precedes it by causality, or by nobility, or by place — all of which are agreed to be invalid — or you mean that non-existence precedes it by nature because the possible is deserving of non-existence from itself and existence from another, and what is by essence is prior to what is by another, or you mean by it precedence in time — this necessitates the eternity of time, because if the concept of that precedence is not first and that concept is a requirement for the actualization of time, then time must not be first, and then from the eternity of time follows the eternity of motion and body as is known. So this interpretation necessitates the eternity of time. And if you interpret temporality (hadath) as being preceded by the existence of God, then if you mean precedence by causality, by nature, or by nobility, all are granted, and precedence by place is agreed to be invalid. But if by time, it necessitates the eternity of time as stated. And if you mean by temporality a third meaning, let them mention it and I will discuss it.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage examines the various possible interpretations of the phrase 'preceded by non-existence' when defining the temporality (huduth) of the world. The author considers different types of precedence: causal (bil-illiyya), nobility (bil-sharaf), place (bil-makan), nature (bil-tab'), and time (bil-zaman). He argues that if one interprets 'preceded by non-existence' as temporal precedence, this leads to the conclusion that time is eternal, because the concept of temporal precedence presupposes time. If time is eternal, then motion and body must also be eternal (since they are inseparably linked to time). This contradicts the intended claim of the opponent that the world is temporal. The author then considers the alternative that 'preceded by the existence of God' is meant, and again shows that temporal interpretation leads to the same problem, whereas other types of precedence are acceptable and do not entail the world's temporality in the required sense. He concludes by inviting the interlocutor to propose a third meaning if they have one.

Interpreting 'Preceded by the Existence of God' in Defining Temporality
Analyzes 'preceded by existence of God' as an alternative definition of temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the same analysis applies when temporality is defined as being preceded by God's existence, and why the temporal interpretation still fails.

Core Idea
Shifting the reference from non-existence to God does not escape the temporal precedence problem.

Having examined the 'preceded by non-existence' definition, the author now considers an alternative: that temporality means being preceded by God's existence. He applies the same distinctions and shows that only temporal precedence is problematic, while other types are acceptable. He then invites the opponent to propose a third meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic for this section carefully, then the translation. Compare with the first part.
Notice the structure is parallel: again listing types of precedence.
2
Identify which types of precedence are accepted
The author accepts causality, nature, and nobility as valid interpretations of 'preceded by God', but rejects place and time.
Why are causality, nature, and nobility acceptable? They do not imply priority of time.
3
Understand the open invitation
The author concludes by challenging the opponent to propose a third meaning if they are not satisfied. This shows the argument is exhaustive.
This is a rhetorical device to close the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان الأول فإما أن تريدوا به أن العدم سابق عليه بالعلية أو بالشرف أو بالمكان والكل باطل بالاتفاق، أو تريدوا بأن العدم سابق عليه بالطبع لأن الممكن المستحق العدم من ذاته والوجود من غيره وما بالذات أسبق ما بالغير أو تريدوا به السبق بالزمان فهذا يوجب قدم الزمان، لأنه إذا لم يكن مفهوم ذلك السبق أول وكان ذلك المفهوم مقتضى تحقق الزمان ازم أن لا يكون الزمان أول، ثم يلزم من قدم الزمان قدم الحركة والجسم على ما هو معلوم فالقول على هذا الوجه يوجب قدم الزمان، وأما إن فسرتم الحدوث بكونه مسبوقاً بوجود الله تعالى فإن أردتم السبق بالعلية أو بالطبع أو بالشرف فالكل مسلم والسبق بالمكان باطل بالاتفاق، وأما بالزمان فإنه يوجب قدم الزمان على ما تقدم، وإن أردتم بالحدوث معنى ثالثا فليذكروه اتكلم عليه .
If it is the first, then either you mean that non-existence precedes it by causality, or by nobility, or by place — all of which are agreed to be invalid — or you mean that non-existence precedes it by nature because the possible is deserving of non-existence from itself and existence from another, and what is by essence is prior to what is by another, or you mean by it precedence in time — this necessitates the eternity of time, because if the concept of that precedence is not first and that concept is a requirement for the actualization of time, then time must not be first, and then from the eternity of time follows the eternity of motion and body as is known. So this interpretation necessitates the eternity of time. And if you interpret temporality (hadath) as being preceded by the existence of God, then if you mean precedence by causality, by nature, or by nobility, all are granted, and precedence by place is agreed to be invalid. But if by time, it necessitates the eternity of time as stated. And if you mean by temporality a third meaning, let them mention it and I will discuss it.

Commentary
This passage examines the various possible interpretations of the phrase 'preceded by non-existence' when defining the temporality (huduth) of the world. The author considers different types of precedence: causal (bil-illiyya), nobility (bil-sharaf), place (bil-makan), nature (bil-tab'), and time (bil-zaman). He argues that if one interprets 'preceded by non-existence' as temporal precedence, this leads to the conclusion that time is eternal, because the concept of temporal precedence presupposes time. If time is eternal, then motion and body must also be eternal (since they are inseparably linked to time). This contradicts the intended claim of the opponent that the world is temporal. The author then considers the alternative that 'preceded by the existence of God' is meant, and again shows that temporal interpretation leads to the same problem, whereas other types of precedence are acceptable and do not entail the world's temporality in the required sense. He concludes by inviting the interlocutor to propose a third meaning if they have one.

Motion and Rest Require Place
Argues that an eternal body is not necessarily moving or resting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attributes of motion and rest are not necessarily applicable to an eternal body like the world, according to a classical kalam argument.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are place-dependent; what has no place cannot move or rest.

In debating the attributes of an eternal body, some assume it must be moving or resting. This lesson examines a crucial objection: motion and rest are defined relative to place, and if the world as a whole is not in any place, these categories simply do not apply. The author presents a detailed verification showing the difficulties in positing a place for the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow from the initial claim to the detailed verification.
The passage uses technical terms like 'motion', 'rest', and 'place'.
2
Identify the core argument
The author grants that the world could be eternal but denies it must be moving or resting. Find the definitions of motion and rest and see how they link to place.
Motion is transition from place to place; rest is stability in one place.
3
Follow the verification (tahqiq)
The author shows that if the world had a place, that place would have to be a body, leading to an infinite regress of bodies. Even if granted, the regress supports the original point that bodies can move.
The verification uses a disjunction: place is either non-existent or existent, etc.
4
Conclude and reflect
The author concludes that the world cannot have a place, so motion and rest are not applicable. Reflect on why this matters for theology.
This argument defends the transcendence of the world beyond physical categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نزلنا عن هذا المقام لكن لا نسلم أن الجسم لو كان قديما لكان إما أن يكون متحركا أو ساكنا، بيانه أن الحركة عبارة عن الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان والسكون هو الاستقرار في المكان الواحد وهذان القمان فرع الحصول في المكان، وعندنا العالم ليس في مكان فيستحيل وصفه بكونه متحركا ولا بكونه ساكنا، تحقيقه أنه لو كان للعالم مكان لكان مكانه إما أن يكون معدوما أو موجودا والأول محال لأن حصول الموجود في المعديم محال، وإن كان موجوداً إما أن يكون مشار إليه بالحس أو لا يكون، فإن كان مشار إليه كان إما متحيزا أو حالا فيه، فلو كان متحيزا أو حالا فيه لكان مكان الجسم جسما وكل جسم تصبح عليه الحركة فإذا تصح الحركة فى مكان المتحرك فلذلك المكان مكان آخر فيفضى إلى وجود أجسام لا نهاية لها وهو وبتقدير تسليمه، فالمقصود حاصل لأنها كلها أجسام وهى قابلة للحركة، وكل منا يتحرك فإنما يتحرك من من إلى مكان، فإذا لكل الأجسام مكان وذلك المكان لا يكون جسما لأن الخيا كل الأجسام لا يكون -ج جمها وإن لم يكن مشار إليه استحان أن يكون مكانا للجسم، لأن مكان الجسم هو الذي يصح أن يتحرك منه وإليه وذلك لا محالة مشار إليه .
We descend from this station, but we do not grant that if the body were eternal, it would necessarily be either moving or resting. Explanation: motion is transition from place to place, and rest is stability in one place, and these two descriptions are branches of being in place. According to us, the world is not in a place, so it is impossible to describe it as moving or resting. Verification: if the world had a place, its place would be either non-existent or existent. The first is impossible because an existent's occurrence in a non-existent is impossible. If it is existent, it is either perceptible by sense or not. If perceptible, it is either a body or an accident in a body. So if it were a body or accident, the place of the body would be a body, and every body can be moved, so motion would be valid in the place of the moving thing, and thus that place would have another place, leading to infinite bodies, and even if granted, the point is achieved because they are all bodies and capable of motion. And everything that moves does so from place to place. So every body has a place, and that place is not a body because [unclear]. But if it is not perceptible, it is impossible for it to be a place for a body, because the place of a body is that from which and to which it can move, and that is necessarily perceptible.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that an eternal body (like the world) need not be characterized as either moving or at rest. The author grants the opponent's claim (nazalna 'an hadha al-maqam) that the world may be eternal but then disputes the necessity of motion or rest. The reasoning is that motion and rest are defined in terms of place: motion is change of place, rest is remaining in one place. These attributes only apply to things that are in a place. However, according to the author, the world as a whole is not in a place (since there is no external space containing it). Therefore, it cannot be described as moving or resting. He then provides a detailed verification (tahqiq) that if the world were in a place, that place would have to exist and be a body, leading to an infinite regress of bodies. Even if that regress is granted, all those bodies would themselves be in places, so the original point — that bodies are capable of motion — remains. Finally, he argues that a place must be perceptible (mushar ilayh) to serve as a place for motion, so the only coherent notion of place is an existent, perceptible body, which again leads to the infinite regress. Hence, the world cannot have a place, and thus motion and rest are not applicable.

Key Terms

السكون
المكان
الجسم
القديم
المشار إليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any object must be either moving or at rest.
Correction: Motion and rest are relational to place; an object not in a place cannot be described as moving or resting.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that an eternal body is not necessarily moving or resting?
A) Because motion and rest require a place, and the world is not in a place
B) Because an eternal body cannot change
C) Because motion and rest are accidental properties that require a subject
D) Because the world is a single body that cannot move

Key Takeaway
An eternal body that is not in a place cannot be described as moving or resting, because motion and rest are inherently relational to place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you unconsciously picture Him in a specific place or direction? What does this lesson demand you to change about how you think of His existence, and what concrete step will you take tonight to correct any spatial assumptions?
?
You claim to believe that Allah is not confined by space. But in your daily worship—your prayers, your supplications—do you ever act as if He is far away, or as if your physical location determines your closeness to Him? How will you cultivate a sense of His presence that is independent of any place?
?
If only created things are subject to motion and rest, and Allah is beyond such categories, does your love for Him depend on your own physical circumstances and movements? Are you more attached to the comfort of your routines and places than to the Creator who transcends them? What will you do to reorient your heart toward the One who has no place?

Refuting the World's Oneness in Eternity
Refutes objection that world was one body in eternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world could not have been a single body in eternity and how its present divisibility proves it was not one.

Core Idea
Division proves the world was never one.

This lesson addresses an objection suggesting the world might have been a single body in pre-eternity. The author uses a logical argument from observed division to refute the claim. Let's examine how the fact that the world is now divided shows it could not have been originally one.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the objection is presented: it says the world might have been one body and motion/rest require two parts.
Note the structure 'لا يقال : لم لا يجوز...' which introduces an objection.
2
Understand the refutation
Read the author's reply: 'لانا نقول: بينا أن الواحد يستحيل أن ينقسم فلما صار العالم منقسما الآن علمنا أنه لم يكن واحداً.' This is the key logical step.
The core premise: the one cannot become divided.
3
Connect premise to conclusion
Reflect on why the indivisibility of the one is assumed and how present division defeats the claim of original unity.
This argument relies on the nature of indivisible unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال العالم لم كان في الأزل جسما واحدا والحركة والسكون بالتفسير الذي ذكرتموه لا يفرض الأعند حصول الجزئين لانا نقول : بينا أن الواحد يستحيل أن ينقسم فلما صار العالم منقسما الآن علمنا أنه لم يكن واحداً . قوله الأزلى نوع الحركة لا شخصها . قلنا هذا باطل ، لأن الحركة ماهيتها بحسب نوعها مركبة من أمر ينقضى ومن أمر حصل فاذا ماهيتها متعلقة بالمسبوقية بالغير وماهية الأزلية منافية لهذا المعنى فالجمع بينهما محال .
It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that the world in eternity was one body, and motion and rest according to the interpretation you mentioned cannot be posited except when two parts exist? For we say: We have shown that the one cannot divide; since the world is now divided, we know that it was not one. He said: The eternal is the type of motion, not its individual instances. We said: This is false, because motion, by its essence according to its type, is composed of something that passes and something that occurs; thus its essence is related to being preceded by another, and the essence of eternality is incompatible with this meaning, so combining them is impossible.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents two final objections and their refutations. The first objection (لا يقال) suggests that the world might have been a single body in eternity, and motion and rest (as defined by contiguity) only apply when there are at least two parts. The author refutes this by stating that it has already been proven that a single indivisible entity cannot become divided; therefore, the fact that the world is now divided proves it was not originally one. The second objection (قوله الأزلى نوع الحركة لا شخصها) argues that what is eternal is the type (naw') of motion, not its particular instances (shakhs). The author rejects this by analyzing the essence of motion: motion by its very nature involves something that passes and something that occurs, implying it is always preceded by something else (another motion). This relational aspect is incompatible with the essence of eternality, which is immutable and not subject to precedence. Therefore, motion cannot be eternal even in type.

Key Terms
الواحد
الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world could have been a single body in eternity but later became divided.
Correction: True oneness is indivisible; thus if the world is now divided, it was never truly one.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that the world was not one in eternity?
A) Because motion requires two parts
B) Because the world is now divided and the one cannot divide
C) Because the world is eternal
D) Because the objection is invalid

Key Takeaway
Present division proves the world was never a single indivisible entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author insists that true oneness cannot divide. In your own life, are there beliefs or commitments you claim as 'one' but which actually contain hidden divisions or contradictions? Will you examine them honestly, or settle for fragments?
?
If the world's division proves it was never one, what does that imply for your understanding of God's absolute oneness? Are you content with a superficial grasp of tawhid, or will you pursue a deeper conviction that cannot be shaken by doubts?

Will you let this truth reshape how you pray, work, and love today?

Refuting the World as One Body in Eternity
Author refutes the claim that the world was one body in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world could not have been a single indivisible body in eternity, based on its current divisibility.

Core Idea
A true unity cannot divide; the world's divisibility proves it was never one.

This brief passage presents a key objection to the argument that the world originated from a single body in eternity. The author counters by invoking a fundamental principle: what is truly one cannot become divided. Since the world is evidently composed of parts, it must have always been composite, implying its temporal origination. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection starts with 'la yuqālu' and the author's response with 'fa innā naqūlu'.
The passage is a classic objection-and-answer format.
2
Identify the core principle
The author states 'the one cannot divide'. This is the key premise. Understand why this is assumed true.
In kalam, a true unity is indivisible.
3
Apply the principle
The world is now divisible (has parts). If it were one in eternity, it would have to remain indivisible, but it is not. Therefore it was never one.
The conclusion follows logically.
4
Reflect on implications
This refutation supports the argument that the world is originated, because if it were eternal and one, it would remain one, but it didn't.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال العالم لم كان في الأزل جسما واحدا والحركة والسكون بالتفسير الذي ذكرتموه لا يفرض إلا عند حصول الجزئين لانا نقول : بينا أن الواحد يستحيل أن ينقسم فلما صار العالم منقسما الآن علمنا أنه لم يكن واحداً .
It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that the world was in eternity a single body, and motion and rest according to your interpretation are not supposed except upon the occurrence of two parts? For we say: We have demonstrated that the one cannot divide. So when the world has now become divisible, we know that it was not one.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This is a short objection and answer. The opponent suggests that in eternity, the world might have been a single undifferentiated body, and motion and rest only appear when parts arise. The author refutes this by stating a principle: a true unity cannot be divided. Since the world is now observed to be divisible (composed of parts), it could not have been a single indivisible entity in eternity. Thus the world must have always been composite, implying it is originated.

Key Terms
واحد
انقسام
جسم
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a body could be one in essence yet divisible.
Correction: The author assumes that a true unity (the one) cannot divide; thus if the world is divisible, it was never one.

Checkpoint
Why does the author deny that the world was a single body in eternity?
A) Because motion and rest require parts.
B) Because the one cannot divide, but the world is now divisible.
C) Because God created it as multiple.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The world's current divisibility proves it was never a single indivisible body in eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know now that the world's divisibility proves it is not eternal—that it had a beginning. But do you live as if this world is permanent? Where in your heart do you treat created things as if they will never end, and what will you do today to remind yourself that only Allah is eternal?
?
The author says 'the one cannot divide.' Allah is Al-Wāḥid, the Absolutely One. Is there any area of your life where you try to serve two masters—where your loyalty is divided between Allah and something else? What step will you take this week to make tawheed real in that area?
?
If the world's current state (divisibility) reveals its true nature (temporal), what does your current state—your habits, attachments, and fears—reveal about your true belief in the Hereafter? Name one change you need to make so your life matches your claim of faith.

The Necessitating Cause vs. Free Agent
Responses to initial objections about the efficient cause and the eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial objection that the efficient cause of originated things could be a necessitating cause (mujib) rather than a free agent (mukhtar), and the theologian's response that this will be refuted later.

Core Idea
Not all efficient causes are necessitating; freedom of the agent is essential.

In defending the creation proof, one must first address basic objections about the nature of the efficient cause. The first objection suggests that the cause of originated things could simply be a necessitating cause (mujib) rather than a free agent (mukhtar). This lesson presents the objection and the theologian's brief response, which promises a later refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector asks: why must the efficient cause of originated things be a free agent? Could it not be a necessitating cause where each prior condition necessitates the next?
Notice the phrasing 'لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر فى الحادث موجبا لا مختارا'.
2
Examine the response
The theologian simply states that the refutation will come later in the section on proving God's power (Qadiriyyah). This shows that some arguments are postponed to their proper context.
The response does not elaborate here, indicating a structured curriculum.
3
Second objection: the eternal as act of a free agent
A second objection asks why the eternal (qadim) cannot be an act of a free agent. The response says this has already been refuted previously.
This ties back to earlier arguments.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice the dialectical style: quick objections met with references to other parts of the discourse. This foreshadows the systematic approach of Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أوله لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر فى الحادث موجبا لا مختاراً ويسكون كل سابق شرطا لحصول اللاحق ان ذلك الموجب قلنا سنقيم الدلالة على فساده في باب إثبات القادرية إن شاء الله تعالى . قوله : لم لا يجوز أن يكون القديم فعلا لفاعل مختار . قلنا : قد تقدم إبطاله . قوله : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ساكنا قلنا لما تقدم قوله على الوجه الأول الامتناع عدم فلا يعلل ، قلنا مماسة الجسم أو مباينته الجسم آخر وصف وجودى لأنه يقتضى الامماسة التي هي وصف عدمى له : يلزم هذا فى صحة العالم . · قلنا : العالم معدوم محض فلا يصح الحكم عليه بالصفة الثبوتية أما ههنا السكون ثبوتى فيصح التقسيم الذي ذكرناه . قوله : علة الحاجة الحدوث. قلنا: بل الإمكان وهو تقدم بيانه . قوله : تعلق قادريته تعالى بايجاد العالم وتعلق علمه بأن العالم سيوجد قديم وقد عدم بعد وجود العالم . قلنا الموجود هو القدرة والعلم وهما باقيان أزلا وأبدا (0)) .
Its beginning: Why is it not permissible that the effect in the originated thing be a necessitating cause (mūjib) and not a free agent (mukhtār), and that every prior be a condition for the occurrence of the subsequent? If that necessitating cause [is assumed], we say: we will establish the proof of its invalidity in the section on proving the Powerful (Qādirīyah), God willing. His statement: Why is it not permissible that the eternal (qadīm) be an act of a free agent? We say: Its refutation has already preceded. His statement: Why is it not permissible that it be at rest (sākin)? We say: Because of what preceded his statement on the first aspect: impossibility (imtinā‘) is non-existence, so it is not caused. We say: The touching (mumāssah) of a body or its separation (mubāyanah) from another body is an existent attribute, because it necessitates non-touching (al-lāmumāssah) which is a non-existent attribute. To him: This necessitates [the same] regarding the soundness of the world. We say: The world is pure non-existence, so it is not valid to judge it with a positive attribute; but here rest (sukūn) is positive, so the division we mentioned is valid. His statement: The cause of need is origination (ḥudūth). We say: Rather possibility (imkān), and its clarification has preceded. His statement: The attachment of His power to bringing the world into existence and the attachment of His knowledge that the world will exist is eternal, and it has ceased after the world's existence. We say: What exists is power and knowledge, and they are eternal without beginning and without end (0).
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage contains a series of objections and concise responses often referring to previous or future proofs. The objections challenge the claim that the efficient cause of originated things must be a free agent, the possibility of the eternal being an act of a free agent, the possibility of eternal rest, and other points. The answers are brief, stating either that the refutation will come later, has already come, or providing a quick logical distinction. For instance, the response to the objection about rest uses the argument that impossibility (imtinā‘) is non-existent and thus not a cause, and that contact or separation from another body is an existent attribute because it is the opposite of non-contact which is non-existent. The passage also includes a discussion on the cause of need, where it asserts that the cause is possibility (imkān), not origination (ḥudūth), and a response to an objection about the eternity of God's power and knowledge.

Key Terms
المؤثر
الموجب
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an efficient cause must be a necessitating cause, i.e., deterministic, and cannot be a free agent.
Correction: The theologian asserts that a free agent is possible and the opposite view will be refuted later.

Checkpoint
How does the theologian respond to the objection that the efficient cause could be a necessitating cause (mujib)?
A) He refutes it immediately with a logical proof.
B) He says the refutation will be given later.
C) He concedes the point.
D) He quotes scripture.

Key Takeaway
The theologian systematically defers refutations to their proper sections, showing a structured approach to Kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult theological question, do you trust that the systematic approach of the scholars will eventually address it, or do you feel the need for an immediate answer? How does this passage challenge your patience and reliance on the scholarly curriculum?
?
Do you instinctively lean towards seeing God as a necessitating cause (like a natural law) or as a fully free agent? Which view makes you feel more accountable for your actions, and why?
?
This passage shows that some objections are postponed to later sections. In your own learning, are you willing to hold your questions and trust the order of study, or do you insist on immediate resolution? What would change if you adopted the theologian's disciplined patience?

The Objection About Eternal Rest and Impossibility
Objection and response regarding the possibility of eternal rest and the nature of impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the eternal could be at rest (sakin), and the complex response involving the nature of impossibility (imtina') as non-existence and the distinction between contact and separation.

Core Idea
Impossibility is non-existence and cannot be a cause; rest is a positive attribute.

A deeper objection questions whether the eternal could be in a state of rest. The response delves into the nature of impossibility and contact, distinguishing between positive and negative attributes. This lesson unpacks the theological reasoning behind the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about rest
The objector asks: why is it not permissible for the eternal to be at rest (sakin)? The answer references a previous point about impossibility.
Note the term 'السكون' (rest) and 'الامتناع' (impossibility).
2
Understand the first part of the response
The theologian says that impossibility (imtina') is non-existence, so it cannot be a cause. This responds to an unstated assumption that 'rest' implies impossibility of motion.
The logic: if rest were impossible, that impossibility is non-existence and thus not a cause.
3
Analyze the argument about contact
The theologian then discusses contact (mumassah) and separation (mubayanah) between bodies. Contact is an existent attribute because it entails the non-existence of non-contact (which is non-existent).
This is a complex point: contact is positive because it prevents non-contact, which is negative.
4
Consider the counter-objection and final answer
A counter-objection says this logic would also apply to the world (if the world were pure non-existence). The response clarifies: the world is pure non-existence, so it cannot have positive attributes; but rest (sukun) is a positive attribute, so the division holds.
This shows the theologian defending his distinction.

The Cause of Need and Eternity of Divine Attributes
Responses to objections about the cause of contingency and the eternity of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the cause of the world's need is origination (huduth) and the counter-response that it is possibility (imkan), as well as an objection about the eternity of God's power and knowledge and the response that they remain eternal.

Core Idea
The world's need for a cause is due to its possibility, not its origination.

The final objections in this series address two key issues: what exactly makes the world contingent (its origination or its possibility), and the apparent problem that if God's power and knowledge are eternal but the world is temporal, then they would cease after creation. This lesson presents the crisp responses.

Guided Path
1
Examine the objection about the cause of need
The objector claims that the cause of the world's need for a creator is its origination (huduth). The theologian responds: it is rather possibility (imkan), and this has been clarified before.
This is a crucial point: contingency (imkan) is the basis for needing a cause, not temporal origination.
2
Understand the objection about power and knowledge
Another objection states: God's power and knowledge are eternal, and His power is attached to bringing the world into existence and His knowledge that it will exist, but after the world exists, this attachment ceases; so power and knowledge seem to change.
The objector implies that if power and knowledge are eternal, their objects must be eternal too.
3
Analyze the response
The theologian answers: what exists (i.e., subsists) is power and knowledge themselves, and they are eternal without beginning and without end. The attachments (ta'alluq) are accidents of the objects, not changes in the attributes themselves.
Power and knowledge are essential attributes; their relations to temporal things do not alter their eternity.

Critique of the definitional basis of motion
Critique of the definitional basis for motion's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author's definition of motion leads to a problem for its claimed existence.

Core Idea
A definition that includes a non-existent part cannot support absolute existence.

The critic begins by questioning the very definition of motion. If motion is defined as 'obtaining in a place after another', then one part of its quiddity—the 'afterness'—is non-existent. This undermines any claim that motion exists absolutely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the first sentence and its translation. Notice the key terms: 'motion' (الحركة), 'quiddity' (الماهية), 'non-existent' (معدوم).
This is a dense line; take your time.
2
Identify the definition problem
The critic argues that motion's quiddity has two parts. One part is non-existent. Ask: what are the two parts?
The part that is non-existent is the 'afterness' or the fact of being in a later place.
3
Connect to the broader claim
Because a part is non-existent, the statement that motion exists absolutely is not correct. This challenges the very possibility of motion being pre-eternal.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this definitional critique undermines the whole discussion about eternal motion.
If the definition is flawed, the arguments built on it are weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير ثبوتية وقد أعلن القول بوجود الحركة فيلزم أن يكون أحمد جزئى ماهيتها معدوما فلا يكون القول بوجودها على الإطلاق صحيحا أما قوله في الوجه الثانى من بيان امتناع كون الحركة أزلية أن كل الحركات محتاج إلى موجد مختار فغير بين بنفسه ولم يو د عليه دليلا وقد يلوح من كلامه عند الاعتراض عليه أنه إنما قيد و موجد المختار لتخلف الحركة عنه و امتناع تخلف المعلول عن العملة الموجبة ، ولكر لو سلم له هذا السلم في كل واحد من الحركات، أما المجموع والنوع فلم يثبت كونهما متخلفين عن مؤثرهما حتى يسوغ له الدلالة بالتخلف على كون الموجد مختارا ، وقد أحال في الجواب بيان امتناع كون الموجد وجبا وكون كل سابق شرطا الحصول اللاحق إلى باب إثبات القادر ، وفى ذلك ام يزد على قوله
Non-existent. And he has declared the statement of the existence of motion, so it follows that one of the two parts of its quiddity is non-existent, so the statement of its existence absolutely is not correct. As for his statement in the second aspect (al-wajh al-thānī) of explaining the impossibility of motion being pre-eternal, that every motion needs a free agent (mūjid mukhtār), it is not evident by itself, and he did not provide a proof for it. It appears from his speech when objecting to him that he only restricted [it to] a free agent because motion lags behind him, and the impossibility of the effect lagging behind the necessitating cause (al-‘illah al-mūjibah). But if this is granted to him for each individual motion, then the totality and the type have not been proven to lag behind their effect so that he could use the lag to indicate that the agent is free. And he referred in the answer to the explanation of the impossibility of the agent being a necessitating cause and that every prior is a condition for the occurrence of the subsequent to the section on proving the Powerful, and in that he did not add to his statement.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage continues the critique of arguments against eternal motion. It first points out that if motion is defined as obtaining in a place after another, then one part of its quiddity (the afterness) is non-existent, so claiming absolute existence of motion is problematic. It then focuses on the second proof: the claim that every motion requires a free agent. The critic argues that this is not self-evident and that even if granted for individual motions, the totality or type of motions has not been shown to depend on an agent in the same way. The author simply refers to another section without providing a conclusive argument here.

Key Terms
الحركة
الماهية
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the statement that motion exists absolutely not correct?
A) Motion is not a real entity
B) One of the two parts of its quiddity is non-existent
C) The author did not provide a proof
D) Motion requires a free agent

Key Takeaway
A definition that includes a non-existent component cannot support a claim of absolute existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a deeply held belief you have about yourself—something you claim to 'be' or to 'have'. Is part of that definition based on something not yet realized? How does that affect the certainty of that belief, and what would it cost you to be honest about it?
?
You likely describe your faith as 'alive' or 'real' in your heart. But if part of that description depends on future actions or feelings you haven't yet actualized, can you truly claim it exists right now? What would you have to change to make the definition match reality today?

Critique of the second proof: every motion needs a free agent
Critique of the argument that every motion requires a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that every motion requires a free agent is insufficient, especially for the totality of motions.

Core Idea
Even if each motion needs a free agent, the totality may not.

The critic now turns to the second proof against eternal motion: the claim that every motion needs a free agent. He argues that this is not self-evident, and even if granted for individual motions, the totality or type of motions has not been shown to depend on an agent in the same way. The author's response only refers to another section without a conclusive argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the passage and its translation. Notice the distinction between individual motions and the totality/type.
Key terms: 'free agent' (موجد مختار), 'lagging behind' (التخلف), 'necessitating cause' (العلة الموجبة).
2
Understand the critic's first point
The critic says the claim that every motion needs a free agent is not evident and not proven. Ask: why would someone think it is obvious?
The author might think that because motion changes, it needs a changer.
3
Grasp the distinction
The critic concedes for argument's sake that each individual motion might need a free agent, but then points out that the totality of motions (the collection or type) has not been shown to lag behind its cause.
Even if each effect has a cause, the whole set may be caused differently.
4
Evaluate the author's response
The author simply refers to another section on proving the Powerful, without adding anything. This is seen as insufficient.
This weakness undermines the second proof against eternal motion.

Refutation of the First Proof Against Eternal Rest
Critique of the first proof against eternal rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of the argument that motion cannot be pre-eternal and the rebuttal based on the possibility of continuous motion and the non-existent nature of impossibility.

Core Idea
Impossibility is non-existent; cannot serve as cause or condition.

This passage begins by addressing the author's first proof against eternal rest, which claimed that motion's actuality depends on the existence of motion itself, making pre-eternal motion impossible. The critic points out that the possibility of continuous motion has already been established, and that impossibility itself is non-existent and cannot be a necessary concomitant. This sets the stage for a deeper critique of the author's causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's first argument
Read the first part of the passage carefully: the author argues that since motion cannot exist in pre-eternity, a body cannot be at rest eternally because rest is defined relative to motion.
Notice how the critic immediately references earlier discussions.
2
Understand the critic's first rebuttal
The critic says the possibility of continuous motion has already been shown, so the proof's foundation collapses.
This is a key point: the possibility of a type of motion suffices.
3
Analyze the second rebuttal
Impossibility (al-imitinā') is non-existent, and the non-existent cannot be a cause, effect, or relation. Thus it cannot be a necessary concomitant.
This challenges the author's causal framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله في الوجه الأول في إبطال القسم الثاني بامتناع كون الجسم في الأزل ساكنا إن صحة الحركة تتوقف وجود الحركة فى نفسها وقد مربيان استحالتها في الأزل فيقال له قد تبين مما مر إمكان استمرار نوع الحركة في الأزل ، وإذا كان كذلك فقد بطل أصل هذا الدليل ، وأيضاً امتناع الحركة لا يكون لذاتها وهو عدمى والعدمى عنده لا يكون علة ولا معلولا ولا مضافا إذ الإضافة عدمية عنده أيضا فلا يكون لازما لمامر، وهو أن اللزوم من غير اعتبار العلمية والمعلولية غير معقول، وأشار إلى ذلك في الاعتراض بقوله الامتناع عدم فلا يعال . وأما قوله في الجواب : إن مماسة الجسم أو مباينته لجسم آخر وصف وجودى لأنه نقيض اللامماسة فنقول عليه قد مر الكلام على هذا التقرير وأيضا المماسة والمباينة إضافيتان وعندك لا شيء من الإضافات موجود وأيضا المسكون ليس إضافيا ، فلا يصح تفسيره بالإضافات . و بوله في الوجه الثاني : إن السكون إن كان أزليا ولم يكن واجبا لذاته افتتر إلى مؤثر موجب ، والموجب إن لم يسكن تأثيره موقوفا على شرط امتنع زواله ، وإن كان موقوفا على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان واجبا امتنع زوال السكون وإن كان ممكنا عاد التقسيم . فيقال له : لا تسلم هذا بعد تسليم كون السكون ثبوتيا إلا بعد بيان امتناع كون كل شرط مشروطا بشرط آخر قبله ، لا إلى أول ولم يوجد ذلك البيان في كلامك . وقوله : من أراد تعميم الدلالة فلا بد له من بيان مماثلة الأجسام ليس بوارد لأن الدليل إن صح دل على امتناع وجود مالا ينفك أما عن الحركه أو عن السكون سواء كان ذلك شيئا واحدا أو أشياء متماثلة أو مختلفة ، ولو ثبت اتفاق الاتصافات بهما أزلا لشيء لا يخلو عنهما لثبت حدوث ذلك الشيء كيف ما كان .
As for his statement in the first aspect (al-wajh al-awwal) of refuting the second division, regarding the impossibility of the body being at rest in pre-eternity: that the soundness of motion depends on the existence of motion in itself, and they have both mentioned its impossibility in pre-eternity. So it is said to him: It has become clear from what preceded the possibility of the continuation of the type of motion in pre-eternity. If that is the case, then the basis of this proof has become invalid. Also, the impossibility of motion is not due to its essence, and it is non-existent, and the non-existent according to him is neither a cause nor an effect nor a relation, since relation is also non-existent according to him. So it cannot be a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of what preceded, and that concomitance without consideration of causality is unintelligible. He indicated this in the objection by saying: impossibility is non-existence, so it is not caused. As for his statement in the answer: that the touching (mumāssah) of a body or its separation (mubāyanah) from another body is an existent attribute because it is the opposite of non-touching (lāmumāssah). We say to him: The discussion on this explanation has already passed. Also, touching and separation are relations (iḍāfāt), and according to you, none of the relations exist. Also, the rested (maskūn) is not relational, so it is not valid to interpret it by relations. And his statement in the second aspect: that if rest (sukūn) is pre-eternal and not necessary by itself, it would need a necessitating cause (mu’aththir mūjib). If the necessitating cause's effect is not dependent on a condition, its removal is impossible. If it is dependent on a condition, then if that condition is necessary, the removal of rest is impossible; if the condition is possible, the division returns. So it is said to him: This cannot be conceded after conceding that rest is positive, except after explaining the impossibility of every condition being conditioned by another condition before it ad infinitum, and that explanation is not found in your speech. And his statement: Whoever wants to generalize the proof must explain the similarity (mumāthalah) of bodies is not relevant, because if the proof is sound, it proves the impossibility of something that is not free from either motion or rest, whether that thing is one or similar or different things. And if the agreement of attributions of both in pre-eternity for something that is not free from them is established, then the origination of that thing would be established however it is.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage critiques the author's proofs against the possibility of eternal rest. The first proof claims that motion's actuality depends on the existence of motion itself, but the critic points out that the possibility of the continuation of the type of motion has already been shown, undermining the proof. Additionally, impossibility is non-existent and cannot be a necessary concomitant. The second proof involves defining touching and separation as positive attributes, but the critic argues they are relations (iḍāfāt) which the author considers non-existent. The third proof argues that if rest is pre-eternal and not necessary, it would need a necessitating cause, leading to an infinite regress of conditions. The critic responds that this regress has not been proven impossible, and the proof does not require demonstrating the similarity of bodies. Finally, the passage concludes that if something is never free from motion or rest in pre-eternity, that thing must be originated.

Key Terms
السكون
الأزل
الحركة
العدم
العلة
اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the critic gives to invalidate the author's first proof?
A) Rest is not defined by motion
B) The possibility of continuous motion has already been established
C) The author did not consider non-existent entities
D) The proof relies on a false analogy

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of motion in pre-eternity is undermined by the possibility of continuous motion, and impossibility itself is non-existent and cannot be a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What assumptions do I hold about things I consider 'impossible' that might actually be based on a misunderstanding of causality? How can I guard against using 'impossibility' as a lazy argument in my own reasoning?
?
Has there ever been a time I rejected a possibility because it seemed impossible, only later to find it was actually possible? How does this lesson challenge my approach to what I think is possible or impossible in matters of faith?
?
The critic argues that impossibility cannot be a cause—do I ever let the fear of what I think is impossible prevent me from seeking truth? How can I apply this lesson to my own pursuit of knowledge about Allah?

Refutation of the Second Proof: Touching and Separation as Relations
Critique of the second proof: defining touching/separation as existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of the author's attempt to define touching and separation as existent attributes, and why they are considered non-existent relations.

Core Idea
Touching and separation are relations, not positive attributes; relations are non-existent.

In this passage, the author attempts to salvage his second proof by arguing that touching (mumāssah) and separation (mubāyanah) are positive, existent attributes because they are opposites of non-touching. The critic responds with three points: the discussion has already been covered, these are relations which the author considers non-existent, and the concept of 'rested' (maskūn) is not relational. This reinforces the theme that many theological concepts are relational and thus not real in the author's ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's counter-argument
The author claims that touching and separation are existent attributes because they are opposites of non-existence.
Note how he tries to define them positively.
2
Understand the critic's first reply
The critic says the discussion on this point has already passed, implying it was settled earlier.
This refers to earlier sections of the work.
3
Analyze the second and third replies
Touching and separation are relations (iḍāfāt), and according to the author's own view, relations do not exist. Also, the term 'rested' is not relational, so interpreting it via relations is invalid.
This undermines the author's definitional move.

?

?

Refutation of the Third Proof: Eternal Rest and the Necessitating Cause
Critique of the third proof: eternal rest and necessitating cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of the argument that eternal rest requires a necessitating cause leading to an infinite regress, and why the critic rejects this as unproven.

Core Idea
The impossibility of an infinite regress of conditions is not proven, so the argument fails.

The author's third proof argues that if rest is pre-eternal and not necessary by itself, it must have a necessitating cause. Depending on whether that cause's effect depends on a condition, we either get eternal rest or an infinite regress. The critic responds that this entire line of reasoning can only be accepted after proving that an infinite regress of conditions is impossible, which the author has not done. Additionally, the critic dismisses the need to prove similarity of bodies, as the proof applies universally.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's argument
The author sets up a dichotomy: if rest is pre-eternal and contingent, it needs a cause. The cause's effect either depends on a condition or not, leading to either impossibility of removal or infinite regress.
This is a standard kalām argument for temporal origination.
2
Learn the critic's main rebuttal
The critic says the whole argument can only be conceded if the impossibility of an infinite regress of conditions is demonstrated, which the author has not done.
This directly targets the author's reliance on an unproven premise.
3
Examine the final generalization
The critic also argues that the proof does not require showing similarity of bodies; if it works, it proves that anything not free from motion or rest must be originated.
This broadens the applicability of the proper proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله في الوجه الأول في إبطال القسم الثاني بامتناع كون الجسم في الأزل ساكنا إن صحة الحركة تتوقف وجود الحركة فى نفسها وقد مربيان استحالتها في الأزل فيقال له قد تبين مما مر إمكان استمرار نوع الحركة في الأزل ، وإذا كان كذلك فقد بطل أصل هذا الدليل ، وأيضاً امتناع الحركة لا يكون لذاتها وهو عدمى والعدمى عنده لا يكون علة ولا معلولا ولا مضافا إذ الإضافة عدمية عنده أيضا فلا يكون لازما لمامر، وهو أن اللزوم من غير اعتبار العلمية والمعلولية غير معقول، وأشار إلى ذلك في الاعتراض بقوله الامتناع عدم فلا يعال . وأما قوله في الجواب : إن مماسة الجسم أو مباينته لجسم آخر وصف وجودى لأنه نقيض اللامماسة فنقول عليه قد مر الكلام على هذا التقرير وأيضا المماسة والمباينة إضافيتان وعندك لا شيء من الإضافات موجود وأيضا المسكون ليس إضافيا ، فلا يصح تفسيره بالإضافات . و بوله في الوجه الثاني : إن السكون إن كان أزليا ولم يكن واجبا لذاته افتتر إلى مؤثر موجب ، والموجب إن لم يسكن تأثيره موقوفا على شرط امتنع زواله ، وإن كان موقوفا على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان واجبا امتنع زوال السكون وإن كان ممكنا عاد التقسيم . فيقال له : لا تسلم هذا بعد تسليم كون السكون ثبوتيا إلا بعد بيان امتناع كون كل شرط مشروطا بشرط آخر قبله ، لا إلى أول ولم يوجد ذلك البيان في كلامك . وقوله : من أراد تعميم الدلالة فلا بد له من بيان مماثلة الأجسام ليس بوارد لأن الدليل إن صح دل على امتناع وجود مالا ينفك أما عن الحركه أو عن السكون سواء كان ذلك شيئا واحدا أو أشياء متماثلة أو مختلفة ، ولو ثبت اتفاق الاتصافات بهما أزلا لشيء لا يخلو عنهما لثبت حدوث ذلك الشيء كيف ما كان .
As for his statement in the first aspect (al-wajh al-awwal) of refuting the second division, regarding the impossibility of the body being at rest in pre-eternity: that the soundness of motion depends on the existence of motion in itself, and they have both mentioned its impossibility in pre-eternity. So it is said to him: It has become clear from what preceded the possibility of the continuation of the type of motion in pre-eternity. If that is the case, then the basis of this proof has become invalid. Also, the impossibility of motion is not due to its essence, and it is non-existent, and the non-existent according to him is neither a cause nor an effect nor a relation, since relation is also non-existent according to him. So it cannot be a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of what preceded, and that concomitance without consideration of causality is unintelligible. He indicated this in the objection by saying: impossibility is non-existence, so it is not caused. As for his statement in the answer: that the touching (mumāssah) of a body or its separation (mubāyanah) from another body is an existent attribute because it is the opposite of non-touching (lāmumāssah). We say to him: The discussion on this explanation has already passed. Also, touching and separation are relations (iḍāfāt), and according to you, none of the relations exist. Also, the rested (maskūn) is not relational, so it is not valid to interpret it by relations. And his statement in the second aspect: that if rest (sukūn) is pre-eternal and not necessary by itself, it would need a necessitating cause (mu’aththir mūjib). If the necessitating cause's effect is not dependent on a condition, its removal is impossible. If it is dependent on a condition, then if that condition is necessary, the removal of rest is impossible; if the condition is possible, the division returns. So it is said to him: This cannot be conceded after conceding that rest is positive, except after explaining the impossibility of every condition being conditioned by another condition before it ad infinitum, and that explanation is not found in your speech. And his statement: Whoever wants to generalize the proof must explain the similarity (mumāthalah) of bodies is not relevant, because if the proof is sound, it proves the impossibility of something that is not free from either motion or rest, whether that thing is one or similar or different things. And if the agreement of attributions of both in pre-eternity for something that is not free from them is established, then the origination of that thing would be established however it is.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage critiques the author's proofs against the possibility of eternal rest. The first proof claims that motion's actuality depends on the existence of motion itself, but the critic points out that the possibility of the continuation of the type of motion has already been shown, undermining the proof. Additionally, impossibility is non-existent and cannot be a necessary concomitant. The second proof involves defining touching and separation as positive attributes, but the critic argues they are relations (iḍāfāt) which the author considers non-existent. The third proof argues that if rest is pre-eternal and not necessary, it would need a necessitating cause, leading to an infinite regress of conditions. The critic responds that this regress has not been proven impossible, and the proof does not require demonstrating the similarity of bodies. Finally, the passage concludes that if something is never free from motion or rest in pre-eternity, that thing must be originated.

Key Terms
السكون
الأزل
الموجب
الشرط
العلة
المماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite regress of conditions is impossible without further proof.
Correction: The impossibility of an infinite regress of conditions must be demonstrated; it is not self-evident.

Checkpoint
What must be proven before the author's third proof can be accepted?
A) That rest is a positive attribute
B) That an infinite regress of conditions is impossible
C) That bodies are similar to each other
D) That motion is possible in pre-eternity

Key Takeaway
The argument from necessitating cause fails unless the impossibility of an infinite regress of conditions is established, which the author did not provide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any beliefs about God or existence solely because they seem intuitive, without having examined whether they rest on unproven assumptions? What would it take for you to honestly test those assumptions?
?
When you encounter a logical argument that you find convincing, do you check whether its premises are truly established, or do you accept them because they feel plausible? How might this lesson change how you evaluate such arguments?
?
Is there an area of your life where you avoid questioning the foundations of your beliefs because doing so would be uncomfortable? Will you commit this week to examining one such belief with the same rigor as the critic examines the author's proof?

Philosophers' Argument from the Agent Cause
Imam Razi presents the philosophers' argument from the agent cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument from the agent cause against the world's creation and why they claim it must be eternal.

Core Idea
An eternal agent cannot prefer one time over another without a preponderant.

Imam Razi presents the philosophers' case that every originated thing needs four causes. Applying this to the world, they argue that if the world were created, its time of creation would require a preponderant, which leads to an impossibility. This passage focuses on the agent cause and the problem of specifying a time without a reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the structure: general principle, then application to the world, then focus on the agent cause.
The passage is from Imam Razi's Muhassal, presenting the philosophers' view.
2
Identify the four causes
The philosophers state that every originated thing has four causes: agent, matter, form, and purpose. Note that only the agent cause is discussed here.
The other three causes are mentioned but not elaborated in this passage.
3
Analyze the agent cause argument
The argument: if the world originated, it has an eternal agent. The time of creation must be specified, either with a preponderant or without. Both options are rejected: with a preponderant is impossible because pure non-existence cannot have distinction; without a preponderant is impossible because the preponderance of one side without a preponderant is impossible.
This leads the philosophers to conclude that the world must be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فقد قالوا كل محدث فلا بد له من علل أربعة الفاعل والمادة والصورة والغاية، قالوا ونحن نبين في هذه الجهات امتناع حدوث العالم. أما بالنظر إلى الفاعل فلأن العالم لو كان محدثا لكان له مؤثر قديم، فتخصيص إحداثه بالوقت الذي أحدثه فيه إما أن يكون لمرجح أو لا المرجح، والأول باطل لأن النفي المحض لا يعقل فيه الامتياز، والثاني باطل لما سبق أن ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير مرجح محال.
And as for the philosophers, they have said: Every originated thing must have four causes: the agent, the matter, the form, and the purpose. They said: We will explain in these respects the impossibility of the world's occurrence. As for the consideration of the agent: because if the world were originated, it would have an eternal effect; then the specification of its bringing into existence at the time in which it was brought into existence is either with a preponderant or without a preponderant. The first is invalid because pure non-existence is not intelligible to have distinction; and the second is invalid because of what has preceded: the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderant is impossible.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument that every originated thing requires four causes (agent, matter, form, purpose). Applying this to the world, they argue that if the world were originated, it would have an eternal agent (God). The specification of the time of creation would require a preponderant factor (something that makes that time preferable). If there is no preponderant, then the time of creation is arbitrary, which is impossible; if there is a preponderant, it would have to be either the agent or something else. But if it is the agent, then the agent must have been able to create from eternity, so why delay? This leads to a regress or the need for an external preponderant. The philosophers conclude that the world cannot be originated; rather, it must be eternal. Imam Razi here is presenting their argument; the response is implied in the broader context of the book.

Key Terms
محدث
علل أربعة
فاعل
مرجح
امتياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument proves the world must be eternal and is valid.
Correction: The argument assumes that a free agent cannot will a specific time without a preponderant, but Islamic theologians argue that God's will is eternal and can differentiate without external preponderant.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' argument, what is the problem with there being no preponderant for the time of creation?
A) Pure non-existence cannot have distinction.
B) The agent would be imperfect.
C) The world would be necessary.
D) The time would be infinite.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use the need for a preponderant to argue that an eternal agent cannot create at a specific time, thus the world must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider God's decision to create the universe at a specific moment, do you truly believe He had absolutely no need or compulsion to do so? How does that absolute freedom confront your own desire to control every detail of your life?
?
The philosophers argue that an eternal agent must have a reason to choose one time over another. Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God owes you an explanation for His actions? How can you train your heart to trust His wisdom without demanding a reason?
?
If the philosophers' argument were true, the world would be eternal and your existence would be necessary, not a gift. How does the belief that God freely willed you into existence affect your gratitude and your sense of purpose today?

Razi’s Response: Intrinsic Possibility vs. Extrinsic Impossibility
Imam Razi responds to the objection that the possibility of the world's existence has no beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi’s refutation of the objection that the world’s possibility implies its eternity, and the key distinction between intrinsic possibility and extrinsic impossibility.

Core Idea
Possibility alone does not guarantee actuality; external factors can prevent it.

In this passage, Imam Razi critiques a philosophical objection that claims the world’s possibility has no beginning, which would imply its eternity. He shows that the opponent’s rejoinder is fallacious, and he provides the correct resolution: intrinsic possibility and extrinsic impossibility are not contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: objection, response, Razi’s critique, and his correction.
The passage is dense but clear.
2
Identify the fallacy
Razi points out that the opponent added the word 'occurrence' to make the argument seem valid. Understand why this addition is a fallacy and how it obscures the real issue.
3
Grasp the key distinction
Razi explains that intrinsic possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) and extrinsic impossibility (al-imtina' bi-ghayr) are not contradictory. This is the heart of the argument.
This distinction is crucial in kalam.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this distinction applies to the question of the world’s eternity. Why does possibility not necessitate eternity? Reflect on the role of a free agent or other factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله في الوجه الأول من المناقضة إن إمكان وجود العالم لا أول له فالقول بأنه ممتنع الوجود في الأزل مناقض له وقوله في الجواب إنه لا بداية لإمكان حدوث العالم، لكن أزليته مع فرض الحدوث محال فزاد في الجواب لفظ الحدوث ليصح له المغالطة ، وكان من الصواب أن يقول الإمكان الذاتي والامتناع بالغير لا يتناقضان وإنما يمتنع وجود العالم أزلا مكانه لاستناده إلى فاعل مختار أو غير ذلك ما يقتضى حدوثه
As for his statement concerning the first face of contradiction: 'The possibility of the world's existence has no beginning' — then the statement that it is impossible to exist eternally contradicts it. And his statement in the answer: 'There is no beginning for the possibility of the world's occurrence, but its eternity upon the assumption of occurrence is impossible' — so he added in the answer the word 'occurrence' to make the fallacy valid. The correct thing would have been to say: 'Intrinsic possibility and extrinsic impossibility do not contradict each other. Rather, the world's existence is prevented from being eternal because it depends on a free agent or something else that necessitates its beginning.'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection raised by an opponent. The opponent had argued that the possibility of the world existing has no beginning, so it is always possible. The response given by the philosophers (or the opponent) was that the possibility of occurrence has no beginning, but the world's being eternal while assuming its occurrence is impossible. Imam Razi criticizes this response as a fallacy because it adds the word 'occurrence' to make the argument appear valid. The correct way to resolve the contradiction is to distinguish between intrinsic possibility (الإمكان الذاتي) and extrinsic impossibility (الامتناع بالغير). They do not conflict: the world is intrinsically possible, but it is impossible for it to be eternal due to its dependence on a free agent or other factors that necessitate its temporality. Hence, the world's possibility does not imply its eternity.

Key Terms
إمكان
امتناع
ذاتي
بالغير
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that possibility always implies actuality at some time, so if the world is possible, it must be eternal or come to exist at some point.
Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that intrinsic possibility can be overridden by extrinsic impossibility. The world is possible in itself, but external factors (like dependence on a free agent) prevent it from being eternal.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi say is the correct way to resolve the contradiction between the possibility of the world's existence and its impossibility in eternity?
A) The world is impossible in itself
B) Intrinsic possibility and extrinsic impossibility do not contradict
C) The world must have a beginning in time
D) The philosophers' argument is valid

Key Takeaway
Intrinsic possibility and extrinsic impossibility are not contradictory; the world is possible in itself but prevented from eternity by external factors like dependence on a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that because something was possible, it should have happened in your life? How does Razi’s distinction help you submit to Allah’s wisdom when possibilities do not become actualities?
?
Think of a situation where you felt that something was impossible for you but you later realized it was only prevented by your own limited perspective. How can trusting in Allah as the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) change your response to what seems possible or impossible?
?
If everything that is intrinsically possible were to exist, the world would be overwhelming and chaotic. In what ways does this lesson challenge you to be grateful that Allah, in His wisdom, prevents certain possibilities from becoming real—even those you may desire?
