Response to the Efficient Cause Proof
Imam Razi's response to the efficient cause argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the argument that the world's creation at a specific time requires a preponderant cause, and how he explains specification through God's will.

Core Idea
God's will is self-sufficient and needs no external preponderant.

In the previous section, philosophers argued that the world's origination at a particular time requires a preponderant (murajjih) to specify that time. Imam Razi responds with an analogy showing that specification can occur without a preponderant, then gives the true answer: the attachment of God's will is the determinant, and it is necessary, so no external preponderant is needed. He also addresses an objection about the distinction of times.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first response carefully. Notice the two-part structure: first an analogy, then the true answer.
The analogy uses the planets and the garment to show that specification is possible without an external cause.
2
Understand the analogy
The planets in the celestial sphere occupy specific positions despite the sphere's simplicity. Similarly, a garment has one side thick and the other thin. These show that specification does not require a preponderant.
3
Grasp the true answer
The real determinant is God's will attached to creating the world at that time. This will is necessary, so it needs no preponderant.
This is the central point: God's will is the ultimate cause.
4
Consider the objection and reply
The objection is that specifying a time requires the existence of other times. Razi replies that just as one time can be distinguished from another without a 'time for time', non-existence can be distinguished from existence without time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول أن اختصاص حدوث العالم بوقته الممين كاختصاص الكواكب بالموضع المعين من الفلك مع كونه بسيطا، واختصاص أحد جانبي المتهم بالثخن المخصوص والجانب الآخر بالرقة ، ثم الجواب الحقيقى أن المقتضى لذلك الاختصاص تعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإحداثه في ذلك الوقت وذلك التعلق عندنا واجب فيستغنى من المرجح لا يقال تخصيص الأحداث بالوقت المعين يستدعى امتياز ذلك الوقت عن سائر الأوقات وهذا يقتضى كون الأوقات موجودة قبل ذلك الحادث . ولانا نقول : كما أنه يجوز امتياز وقت عن وقت وإن لم يكن للوقت وقت آخر ، فلم لا يجوز امتیاز العدم عن الوجود من غير وجود الوقت . وعن الثاني : أن يكون الإمكان ليس وصفا وجوديا على مامر ، وأيضا فالمادة ممكنة فيلزم أن يقوم إمكانها بمادة أخرى وهو محال . فإن قلت المادة قديمة فإمكانها قائم بها أما إمكان الحادث لا يمكن قيامه به لاستحالة قيام الموجود بالمعدوم. قلت لو قام إمكان المادة بها لكان وجود المادة شرطا في إمكانها ، لأن وجود المحل شرط في وجود الحال فلو كان إمكان المادة قائمة بها لكان إمكانها مشروطا بوجودها ، لكن وجودها عرض مفارق والموقوف على العرض المفارق مفارق فالإمكان عرض مفارق هذا خلف . وعن الثالث أنك إذا قلت كل محدث فعدمه سابق على وجوده فقد اعترفت بسكون المعدوم موصوفا بالسابقية فوصف العدم لا يجوز أن يكون موجودا لاستحالة قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، فثبت أن السابقية ليست صفة وجودية فيبطل كلامكم بالكلية. وعن الرابع : أنا سنبين أنه تعالى فاصل مختار إن شاء الله العزيز
The answer to the first: the specification of the world's origination to a particular time is like the specification of the planets to a particular position in the celestial sphere despite its simplicity, and the specification of one side of the garment to a particular thickness and the other side to thinness. Then the true answer is that the determinant for that specification is the attachment of the will of God Almighty to bringing it about in that time, and that attachment is necessary according to us, so it is independent of a preponderant. It cannot be said that the specification of events to a particular time requires the distinction of that time from other times, and this necessitates the existence of times before that event. But we say: just as it is permissible for one time to be distinguished from another time even though time has no other time, why is it not permissible for non-existence to be distinguished from existence without the existence of time? As for the second: possibility is not an existential attribute, as previously mentioned. Also, matter is possible, so its possibility would need another matter, which is impossible. If you say: matter is eternal, so its possibility subsists in it; but the possibility of the originated thing cannot subsist in it because it is impossible for an existent to subsist in a non-existent. I say: if the possibility of matter subsisted in it, then the existence of matter would be a condition for its possibility, because the existence of the substrate is a condition for the existence of the attribute. So if the possibility of matter subsisted in it, its possibility would be conditioned by its existence. But its existence is an accident that is separable, and what depends on a separable accident is separable, so possibility would be a separable accident – this is contradictory. As for the third: when you say 'every originated thing, its non-existence is prior to its existence', you have admitted that non-existence is described with priority. But it is not permissible for an attribute of non-existence to be existent, because it is impossible for an existent to subsist in a non-existent. Thus, it is established that priority is not an existential attribute, so your statement collapses entirely. As for the fourth: we will show that He, exalted, is a free agent, if God Almighty wills.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage contains Imam Razi's responses to the four philosophical proofs (efficient, material, formal, final) just presented. The response to the first (efficient cause) has two parts: first, an analogy that specification without a preponderant is possible (like the planets' positions or the garment's thickness). Second, the true answer: the world's creation at a particular time is due to the attachment of God's will, which is necessary and thus does not require an external preponderant. The objection that this would require distinction of times is rejected: just as one time can be distinct from another without a time for time, non-existence can be distinct from existence without time. The response to the second (material cause) argues that possibility is not an existential attribute (as previously established), so it does not require a subject. Even if matter is possible, its possibility would need another matter ad infinitum, which is absurd. If one claims that matter is eternal and its possibility subsists in it, then the existence of matter would be a condition for its possibility, but matter's existence is an accidental attribute, leading to a contradiction. The response to the third (formal cause) denies that priority of non-existence over existence is an existential attribute; it is merely a mental consideration. To describe non-existence as 'prior' does not imply an existent attribute. The response to the fourth (final cause) simply states that God is a free agent, to be explained later.

Key Terms
إرادة
مرجح
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The specification of the world's creation time requires an external preponderant cause.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that God's will, being necessary, is sufficient to specify the time without any external preponderant.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's response to the first argument, what is the true determinant of the world's creation at a specific time?
A) A preponderant external cause
B) The intrinsic nature of matter
C) The attachment of God's will
D) The priority of non-existence

Key Takeaway
God's will, being necessary, is the sufficient determinant for the specific time of the world's creation, without need for an external preponderant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the precise timing of your own life events, do you truly accept that God's will alone is sufficient, or do you subconsciously seek a 'reason' in worldly circumstances to feel in control?
?
If God's will is necessary and independent of any external preponderant, what does that mean for your tendency to question His decisions when they don't align with your expectations?
?
Are you willing to stand before your Creator and admit that your trust in His will is often conditional on your own understanding of causes—or will you let this lesson push you to surrender that need for explanation?

Response to the Material Cause Proof
Imam Razi's response to the material cause argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that possibility is not an existential attribute and therefore does not require a material substrate, refuting the philosophers' material cause proof.

Core Idea
Possibility is not an existential attribute; it does not inhere in a subject.

The philosophers argued that the world's possibility requires a material substrate (matter) to receive that possibility. Imam Razi responds by reminding that possibility (imkān) is not an existential attribute (waṣf wujūdī), so it does not need a subject. He then argues that even if matter were possible, its possibility would require another matter ad infinitum. He also refutes the objection that matter is eternal and its possibility is in itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second response carefully. Note the key claim: possibility is not an existential attribute.
2
Understand the first argument
Razi states that possibility is not existential (as established earlier). Also, if matter is possible, its possibility would need another matter, leading to infinite regress.
3
Examine the objection and reply
Objection: matter is eternal, so its possibility subsists in itself, unlike originated things. Razi's reply: if possibility subsisted in matter, then matter's existence would be a condition for its possibility, but matter's existence is an accidental attribute, so possibility would be accidental, leading to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول أن اختصاص حدوث العالم بوقته الممين كاختصاص الكواكب بالموضع المعين من الفلك مع كونه بسيطا، واختصاص أحد جانبي المتهم بالثخن المخصوص والجانب الآخر بالرقة ، ثم الجواب الحقيقى أن المقتضى لذلك الاختصاص تعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإحداثه في ذلك الوقت وذلك التعلق عندنا واجب فيستغنى من المرجح لا يقال تخصيص الأحداث بالوقت المعين يستدعى امتياز ذلك الوقت عن سائر الأوقات وهذا يقتضى كون الأوقات موجودة قبل ذلك الحادث . ولانا نقول : كما أنه يجوز امتياز وقت عن وقت وإن لم يكن للوقت وقت آخر ، فلم لا يجوز امتیاز العدم عن الوجود من غير وجود الوقت . وعن الثاني : أن يكون الإمكان ليس وصفا وجوديا على مامر ، وأيضا فالمادة ممكنة فيلزم أن يقوم إمكانها بمادة أخرى وهو محال . فإن قلت المادة قديمة فإمكانها قائم بها أما إمكان الحادث لا يمكن قيامه به لاستحالة قيام الموجود بالمعدوم. قلت لو قام إمكان المادة بها لكان وجود المادة شرطا في إمكانها ، لأن وجود المحل شرط في وجود الحال فلو كان إمكان المادة قائمة بها لكان إمكانها مشروطا بوجودها ، لكن وجودها عرض مفارق والموقوف على العرض المفارق مفارق فالإمكان عرض مفارق هذا خلف . وعن الثالث أنك إذا قلت كل محدث فعدمه سابق على وجوده فقد اعترفت بسكون المعدوم موصوفا بالسابقية فوصف العدم لا يجوز أن يكون موجودا لاستحالة قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، فثبت أن السابقية ليست صفة وجودية فيبطل كلامكم بالكلية. وعن الرابع : أنا سنبين أنه تعالى فاصل مختار إن شاء الله العزيز
The answer to the first: the specification of the world's origination to a particular time is like the specification of the planets to a particular position in the celestial sphere despite its simplicity, and the specification of one side of the garment to a particular thickness and the other side to thinness. Then the true answer is that the determinant for that specification is the attachment of the will of God Almighty to bringing it about in that time, and that attachment is necessary according to us, so it is independent of a preponderant. It cannot be said that the specification of events to a particular time requires the distinction of that time from other times, and this necessitates the existence of times before that event. But we say: just as it is permissible for one time to be distinguished from another time even though time has no other time, why is it not permissible for non-existence to be distinguished from existence without the existence of time? As for the second: possibility is not an existential attribute, as previously mentioned. Also, matter is possible, so its possibility would need another matter, which is impossible. If you say: matter is eternal, so its possibility subsists in it; but the possibility of the originated thing cannot subsist in it because it is impossible for an existent to subsist in a non-existent. I say: if the possibility of matter subsisted in it, then the existence of matter would be a condition for its possibility, because the existence of the substrate is a condition for the existence of the attribute. So if the possibility of matter subsisted in it, its possibility would be conditioned by its existence. But its existence is an accident that is separable, and what depends on a separable accident is separable, so possibility would be a separable accident – this is contradictory. As for the third: when you say 'every originated thing, its non-existence is prior to its existence', you have admitted that non-existence is described with priority. But it is not permissible for an attribute of non-existence to be existent, because it is impossible for an existent to subsist in a non-existent. Thus, it is established that priority is not an existential attribute, so your statement collapses entirely. As for the fourth: we will show that He, exalted, is a free agent, if God Almighty wills.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage contains Imam Razi's responses to the four philosophical proofs (efficient, material, formal, final) just presented. The response to the first (efficient cause) has two parts: first, an analogy that specification without a preponderant is possible (like the planets' positions or the garment's thickness). Second, the true answer: the world's creation at a particular time is due to the attachment of God's will, which is necessary and thus does not require an external preponderant. The objection that this would require distinction of times is rejected: just as one time can be distinct from another without a time for time, non-existence can be distinct from existence without time. The response to the second (material cause) argues that possibility is not an existential attribute (as previously established), so it does not require a subject. Even if matter is possible, its possibility would need another matter ad infinitum, which is absurd. If one claims that matter is eternal and its possibility subsists in it, then the existence of matter would be a condition for its possibility, but matter's existence is an accidental attribute, leading to a contradiction. The response to the third (formal cause) denies that priority of non-existence over existence is an existential attribute; it is merely a mental consideration. To describe non-existence as 'prior' does not imply an existent attribute. The response to the fourth (final cause) simply states that God is a free agent, to be explained later.

Key Terms
إمكان
وصف وجودي
مادة
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is an attribute that inheres in a subject like material substance.
Correction: Imam Razi holds that possibility is not an existential attribute; it is a mental consideration and does not require a subject.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi assert about the nature of possibility (imkān) in his response to the material cause proof?
A) It is an existential attribute that requires a subject
B) It is a mental consideration and not an existential attribute
C) It exists in matter eternally
D) It is a separable accident

Key Takeaway
Possibility is not an existential attribute; therefore, it does not require a material substrate, undermining the material cause proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on material explanations for things you cannot see, does Razi's argument unsettle your confidence? What would it take for you to let go of that reliance?
?
If possibility does not require a material substrate, then what does it mean for your own potential to change and grow? Are you treating your own possibilities as if they depend on circumstances rather than on the Creator's will?
?
Razi shows that assuming possibility inheres in matter leads to a contradiction. Are there beliefs you hold about yourself or the world that seem reasonable but, when examined closely, lead to a contradiction with your faith? Will you examine one today?

Response to the Formal Cause Proof
Imam Razi's response to the formal cause argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that the priority of non-existence over existence is not an existential attribute, refuting the formal cause proof.

Core Idea
Priority is not an existential attribute; describing non-existence with priority is merely a mental construct.

The philosophers argued that the formal cause of the world is the removal of non-existence (ʿadam) prior to existence. Imam Razi responds by pointing out that if one says 'its non-existence is prior', one describes non-existence with priority. But an attribute of non-existence cannot be existent, because an existent cannot subsist in a non-existent. Therefore, priority is not an existential attribute, and the argument collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the third response carefully. It is a short but decisive argument.
2
Identify the assumption
The philosophers' statement 'every originated thing, its non-existence is prior' implicitly attributes priority to non-existence.
3
Understand the refutation
Razi argues that attribution priority to non-existence is impossible if priority is a real attribute, because it would require an existent attribute subsisting in non-existence. Therefore, priority must be a mental consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Response to the Final Cause Proof: God as Free Agent
Imam Razi's response to the final cause argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's assertion that God is a free agent, which refutes the philosophers' argument from final cause.

Core Idea
God is a free agent, acting without need for an external final cause.

The philosophers argued that the world's order demonstrates a final cause (purpose) that necessitates an external goal. Imam Razi's response is to assert that God is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār), meaning His actions are not driven by external purposes. This concept is central to Islamic theology and will be elaborated later. The passage acknowledges that the full proof will come later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the fourth response: a brief statement that we will show God is a free agent.
This is a promise of a future proof.
2
Understand the concept of free agent
In Islamic theology, a free agent acts by His own will without external compulsion or purpose. This contrasts with the philosophers' view that God acts for an end.
3
Connect to the final cause argument
If God is a free agent, then the world's order does not require an external final cause; God's will is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does that reveal a hidden demand in your heart for God to justify Himself to you? What would it look like for you to trust His free will without demanding explanations?
?
If God's actions are not driven by any need or external cause, then every trial and blessing in your life comes purely from His free will. Does that thought fill you with awe or discomfort? Are you willing to surrender your own desire to find 'reasons' behind your circumstances and instead submit to His sovereign choice?
?
The philosophers argued that the world must have a purpose outside of God. Razi’s response redirects that purpose back to God’s free will. In your own life, when you seek purpose, do you look for it outside of God—in achievements, relationships, or circumstances—or do you rest in the fact that being a creature of the Free Agent is itself your ultimate purpose? How can you live today as someone whose sole purpose is to reflect the will of the One who needs no purpose outside Himself?

The Objection of the Specific Time of Creation and Its Resolution
Imam Razi presents the first objection about the specific time of creation and offers multiple answers, culminating in the denial of pre-temporal time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that all times are equal and how Imam Razi resolves it by denying the existence of time before creation.

Core Idea
Time begins with creation; there is no pre-temporal time to choose from.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a classical objection: if God's will is eternal, why did He create the world at a particular moment rather than earlier or later? The objection assumes that all moments are equally possible. Razi presents several answers, ultimately showing that the question presupposes a time that does not exist. This lesson unpacks this deep theological and philosophical argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection states that if all times are equal, a specific time requires a preponderant. Recognize why this is a challenge to divine will.
The objection is based on the principle of sufficient reason.
2
Examine the first answer
Razi first uses an analogy: the specification of planets to positions in the sphere. Notice that this analogy only works within an already existing framework.
3
Analyze the 'true answer'
Razi says the divine will itself determines the time without external preponderant. He calls this the true answer, but later critiques it.
This answer is considered incomplete because it does not address the sub-objection.
4
Follow the sub-objection and response
A critic objects that specifying a time requires that time exists before the event. Razi first responds that just as one time can be distinct without a meta-time, non-existence and existence can be distinct without time. Then he defends this.
This response is also contested.
5
Grasp the final correct answer
The correct answer: times before creation are non-existent and only imagined; time begins with the world. Thus the objection is based on an imaginary presupposition.
This is the resolution that Imam Razi endorses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول أن اختصاص حدوث العالم بوقته الممين كاختصاص الكواكب بالموضع المعين من الفلك مع كونه بسيطا، واختصاص أحد جانبي المتهم بالثخن المخصوص والجانب الآخر بالرقة ، ثم الجواب الحقيقى أن المقتضى لذلك الاختصاص تعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإحداثه في ذلك الوقت وذلك التعلق عندنا واجب فيستغنى من المرجح
لا يقال تخصيص الأحداث بالوقت المعين يستدعى امتياز ذلك الوقت عن سائر الأوقات وهذا يقتضى كون الأوقات موجودة قبل ذلك الحادث . ولانا نقول : كما أنه يجوز امتياز وقت عن وقت وإن لم يكن للوقت وقت آخر ، فلم لا يجوز امتیاز العدم عن الوجود من غير وجود الوقت . والجواب : بأنه كاختصاص الكوكب بموضع من الفلك دون موضع واختصاص ثخن المتمم بجانب دون جانب فغير مفيد ، لأن في الأمور الموجودة يمكن أن يقال المربح هناك موجود وليس بمعلوم وأما في الأمور المدمية فلا يمكن ذلك . وقوله في الجواب الحقيقى : بأن إرادة الله تعالى تتعلق بأحد الوقتين تعلما واجبا من غير احتياج إلى مرجح دعوى مجردة عن الحجة ، والاعتراض عليه بأن القول بالترجيح يستدعى وجود الأوقات صحيح . والجواب : أن الامتياز هناك كما لا يقتضى أن يكون للوقت كذلك لا يقتصى في امتياز العدم عن الوجود أن يكون لها وقت ليس بجواب عنه ، وقد مر الكلام في كون الوقتين غير محتاجين إلى وقت آخر والعدم والوجود محتاجان إلى وقت غيرهما . والجواب الصحيح أن يقال الأوقات التي يطلب فيها الترجيح معدومة لاتمايز بينها إلا فى الوهم وأحكام الوهم فى أمثال ذلك غير مقبولة إنما يبتدى وجود الزمان مع أول وجود العالم ولا يمكن وقوع ابتداء سائر الموجودات قبل ابتداء وجود الزمان أصلا .
And the answer to the first [objection] is that the specification of the world's origination to its designated time is like the specification of the planets to a particular position in the sphere despite its simplicity, and the specification of one of the two sides of the [؟] sphere to a particular thickness and the other side to thinness. Then the true answer is that the determinant for that specification is the attachment of the will of God, the Exalted, to bringing it into existence in that time, and that attachment, according to us, is necessary, so it dispenses with a preponderant.
It should not be said that the specification of originated things to a particular time necessitates the distinction of that time from other times, and this requires that times existed before that event. And we say: just as the distinction of a time from another time is permissible even though time does not have another time, why then is not the distinction of non-existence from existence permissible without the existence of time?
And the answer [to the objection] is that it is like the specification of a planet to a position in the sphere rather than a position, and the specification of the thickness of the [؟] sphere to one side rather than the other; but this is not useful, because in existing things it can be said that the preponderant there exists but is not known, while in non-existent things that is not possible.
And his saying in the true answer that the will of God attaches to one of the two times necessarily without need for a preponderant is a claim devoid of proof. The objection that the statement about preponderance requires the existence of times is correct. And the answer: the distinction there, just as it does not require that time have [another] time, similarly it does not require in the distinction of non-existence from existence that they have a time; this is not an answer to it. The discourse has passed that the two times do not need another time, while non-existence and existence need another time. The correct answer is to say: the times in which preponderance is sought are non-existent; there is no distinction between them except in the imagination, and the judgments of the imagination in such matters are not acceptable. Time only begins with the first existence of the world, and it is impossible for the beginning of other existents to occur before the beginning of time at all.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage deals with the first objection: how can a specific time be chosen for the world's creation when all times are equal? The author first gives a brief answer comparing the specification to the distribution of planets in the sky or the thickness of a sphere. Then he provides the 'true answer' that the divine will determines the time without needing an external preponderant. He then addresses an internal objection: that specifying a time requires times before the event. The response is that just as time itself can be distinguished from another time without requiring a meta-time, non-existence can be distinguished from existence without requiring a separate time. The discussion continues with a critique of the initial answers and finally presents the correct answer: that time originates with the world; before creation there is no time, so the question of preponderance is imaginary.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is a container that exists even if nothing exists, so there are many equal moments before creation.
Correction: Time begins with the world; before creation, there is no time, so the question of a specific time is based on an imaginary presupposition.

Checkpoint
According to the correct answer in this passage, why is the objection about the specific time of creation invalid?

B) Because the divine will is sufficient and needs no preponderant.
C) Because times before creation are non-existent and only imagined; time begins with the world.
D) Because the world is eternal and did not have a beginning.

Key Takeaway
The objection that a specific time requires a preponderant fails because time itself does not exist before creation; there is no plurality of times to choose from.

God's Will and Time: Two Answers to a Kalam Objection
Author gives two answers to the objection that God's will needs a time for preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that time is not a prerequisite for God's will and that time itself begins with the creation of the world.

Core Idea
Time begins with creation; God's will needs no time for preference.

This passage tackles a critical objection to the doctrine of creation ex nihilo: if God could have created the world at any time, why did He choose this particular moment? The objection demands a preponderating factor in time. The author examines two attempted answers and presents the correct one, which ties the origin of time itself to the first moment of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation. Let the flow of the argument come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the objection and the two answers
Find the objection (why did God choose this time?) and note that two answers are given. The first is dismissed; the second is discussed and then corrected.
The first answer is compared to a star's position; the second is called 'the true answer'.
3
Understand key terms
Focus on tarjih (preference), waqt (time), and wahm (imagination). These are central to the argument.
The term 'وهم' (wahm) refers to estimative faculty or imagination.
4
Grasp the final correct answer
The author concludes that times before creation are non-existent and distinct only in imagination. Time itself begins with the first existence of the world.
This refutes the need for a preponderant existing in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : بأنه كاختصاص الكوكب بموضع من الفلك دون موضع واختصاص ثخن المتمم بجانب دون جانب فغير مفيد ، لأن في الأمور الموجودة يمكن أن يقال المربح هناك موجود وليس بمعلوم وأما في الأمور المدمية فلا يمكن ذلك . وقوله في الجواب الحقيقى : بأن إرادة الله تعالى تتعلق بأحد الوقتين تعلما واجبا من غير احتياج إلى مرجح دعوى مجردة عن الحجة ، والاعتراض عليه بأن القول بالترجيح يستدعى وجود الأوقات صحيح . والجواب : أن الامتياز هناك كما لا يقتضى أن يكون للوقت كذلك لا يقتصى في امتياز العدم عن الوجود أن يكون لها وقت ليس بجواب عنه ، وقد مر الكلام في كون الوقتين غير محتاجين إلى وقت آخر والعدم والوجود محتاجان إلى وقت غيرهما . والجواب الصحيح أن يقال الأوقات التي يطلب فيها الترجيح معدومة لاتمايز بينها إلا فى الوهم وأحكام الوهم فى أمثال ذلك غير مقبولة إنما يبتدى وجود الزمان مع أول وجود العالم ولا يمكن وقوع ابتداء سائر الموجودات قبل ابتداء وجود الزمان أصلا
And the answer: that it is like the specificity of a star to a position in the sphere without another position and the specificity of the thickness of the supplement to a side without a side is not useful, because in existing things it can be said that the preference there exists and is not known, but in non-existent things this is not possible. And his statement in the true answer: that the will of God Most High is attached to one of the two times by necessary knowledge without need for a preponderant is a claim devoid of proof, and the objection to it that the statement of preference necessitates the existence of times is correct. And the answer: that the distinction there, just as it does not require that there be a time, likewise does not require that in the distinction of non-existence from existence there be a time; this is not an answer to it, and the discussion has already passed regarding the two times not needing another time, and non-existence and existence needing another time besides them. And the correct answer is that the times in which preference is sought are non-existent, with no distinction between them except in imagination (al-wahm), and the judgments of imagination in such matters are not accepted; time only begins with the first existence of the world, and it is impossible for the beginning of other existents to occur before the beginning of time at all.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents two attempts to answer an objection about God's will requiring a time for preference. The objection is that if God chooses one of two equally possible times for creation, there must be some preponderating factor or time. The first answer is dismissed as unhelpful—it compares the case to a star's specific position, but that analogy fails because in existing things the preference is known, whereas in non-existent things (like times before creation) there is no such known preference. The second answer is called 'the true answer'. It begins by rejecting the idea that God's will needs any external preponderant; rather, His will attaches necessarily to one time. The objector counters that 'preference' itself implies the existence of times. The author responds that the distinction between the two times does not require a time any more than the distinction between existence and non-existence requires a time. Ultimately, the correct answer is that before creation, times do not exist; they are purely imaginary with no real distinctness. Time begins only with the first existence of the world (the universe). Therefore, there is no need for a preponderant that exists in time, because time itself originates with the world.

Correction: The passage shows that time itself begins with creation; before that, there is no real time, only imaginary distinctions.

Objection and Answer: Precedence of Non-Existence
Author responds to the claim that non-existence's precedence requires another temporal thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection regarding the precedence of non-existence over existence and the attempted answer that it is merely mental.

Core Idea
The precedence of non-existence is mental, not real.

Imam Razi presents the third objection against a previous argument. The objection claims that the precedence of non-existence would require another temporal thing. The answer, though corrupt, suggests that this precedence is merely mental (dhihnī). We will examine this objection and answer carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the third objection: that the precedence of non-existence over existence necessitates another temporal thing.
The objection aims to show a regress.
2
Read the answer
Read the answer: the precedent is not existential; the precedence is mental, resulting from imagining prior eternity.
The text is corrupt, so focus on the core idea.
3
Reflect on the corruption
Note that the Arabic text is heavily corrupt, indicated by [؟]. The exact meaning is uncertain, but the answer likely denies real temporal precedence.
4
Consider the implications
Think about what it means for precedence to be mental rather than real. How does this answer the objection?
This connects to the broader discussion of time and existence.

والتشكيك الثالث : بأن سبق العدم على الوجود يقتضى وجود حادث مثل ذلك الحادث. والجواب بأن السابق ليس ثبوتيا [؟] أيضا ليس [؟] [لاسم يسترفون] بأن ذلك السبق ذهنى يلزم من توهم القدم السابق إلا أنه وجود زمان عندهم يقع فيه العدم السابق والوجود المسبوق ، وهو لم يبطل ذلك

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and a somewhat corrupt answer. The objection states that the precedence of non-existence over existence would require another temporal thing (a time) that itself precedes, leading to a regress. The answer as preserved seems to argue that this precedence is merely mental (dhihnī), not real, arising from imagining an eternal prior. It notes that according to some, there is a time (possibly imaginary) in which non-existence and existence are located, but that this does not disprove the argument. Proving the Finitude of Past Events
Author presents the philosophers' proof for finitude of past events, an objection, and his own solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument for the finitude of past events, the objection based on imagination, and the author's resolution using the real relations of priority and posteriority.

Core Idea
Past events can be proven finite without relying on imagination.

The philosophers proposed a proof for the finitude of past events by applying two series starting from different points. Opponents objected that this application is imaginary. The author resolves the issue by showing that the real relations of 'prior' and 'posterior' suffice, making the proof sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: philosophers' proof, objection, refined argument, counter-objection, author's solution.
The passage uses technical terms like 'تطبيق' (application) and 'سابق/لاحق' (prior/posterior).
2
Identify the key arguments
Locate the philosophers' first argument (each event originated, so whole originated) and the objection that the whole may differ from parts. Then find the investigators' refined argument using application (taṭbīq) and the opponent's objection that this application is imaginary.
The author presents each step with 'قالوا' (they said) and 'اعترض' (objected).
3
Understand the author's resolution
Focus on the author's own solution starting with 'وأنا أقول'. He uses the concepts of 'سابق' (prior) and 'لاحق' (posterior) that every event possesses. By considering events in both aspects, he deduces finitude without needing imaginary application.
The key move is that the priors and posteriors coincide in existence, so finiteness follows.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this resolution is important: it defends the classical proof for the world's temporality (ḥudūth al-‘ālam) against a strong objection. It shows how careful conceptual analysis can resolve apparent difficulties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول : أنه الدليل الذي اعتمد عليه جمهور المتكلمين في هذه المسألة يحتاج إلى إقامة حجة على دعوى واحدة من الدعاوى الأربع المذكورة وهو امتناع وجود حوادث لا أول لها في جانب الماضى فنورد أولا ما قيل فيه وعليه ثم أذكر ما عندى فيه فأقول . الأوائل قالوا في وجوب تناهى الحوادث الماضية : أنه لما كان كل واحد منها حادثا كان الكل حادثا . اعترض عليه بأن حكم الكل ربما يخالف الحكم على الآحاد . ثم قالوا الزيادة والنقصان يطرآن إلى الحوادث الماضية فتكون متناهية، وعرض معلومات الله تعالى ومقدوراته فإن الأولى أكثر من الثانية مع كونهما غير متناهيين . ثم قال المحصلون منهم الحوادث الماضية إذا أخذت تارة مبتدأة من الآن ذاهبة في الماضى وتارة مبتدأة من مثل هذا الوقت من السنة الماضية ذاهبة في الماضى وأطبقت إحداهما على الأخرى في الوهم ، بأن يجعل المبدأ واحدا وهما في الذهاب إلى الماضى متطابقان استحال تساويهما ، وإلا كان وجود الحوادث الواقعة في الزمان الذي هو الآن وهى السنة الماضية وعدمها واحدا ، واستحال كون المبتدأة من السنة الماضية زائدة على المبتدأة من الآن لأن ما ينقص من المتساويين لا يكون زائدا على كل واحد منهما ، فيجب أن تكون المبتدأة من السنة الماضية في جانب الماضى أنقص من المبتدأة من الآن في ذلك الجانب ، ولا يمكن ذلك إلا بانتهائها قبل انتهاء المبتدأة من الآن ويكون الأنقص متناهيا والزائد عليه بمقدار متناه يكون متناهيا فيكون الكل متناهيا . واعتراض الخصم عليهم بأن هذا التطبيق لا يقع إلا في الوهم وذلك يكون بشرط ارتسام المطابقين فيه وغير المتناهى لا يرتسم في الوهم، ومن البين أنهما لا يحصلان في الوجود فضلا عن توهم التطبيق فيهما في الوجود، فهذا الدليل موقوف على حصول ما لا يحصل لا في الوهم ولا في الوجود. وأيضاً الزيادة والنقصان إنما فرض في الطرف المتناهى لا في الطرف الذي وقع النزاع في تناهيه فهو غير مؤثر فيه. فهذا حاصل كلام، في هذا الموضع . وأنا أقول : إن كل حادث موصوف بكونه سابقا على ما بعده ويكون لاحقا لما قبله والاعتباران مختلفان ، فإذا اعتبرنا الحوادث الماضية المبتدأة من الآن تارة من حيث كل واحد منها سابق وتارة من حيث هو بعينه لاحق كانت السوابق واللواحق المتباينين بالاعتبار متطابقين في الوجود ولا يحتاج في تطابقهما إلى توهم تطبيق ومع ذلك يجب كون السوابق أكثر من اللواحق في الجانب الذي وقع النزاع فيه ، إذ اللواحق متناهية في الماضى لوجوب انقطاعها قبل انقطاع السوابق ، والسوابق الزائدة عليها بمقدار متناه متناهية أيضا. فقد تم الدليل في سقوط ما اعترض عليه منه ويتم بذلك الدليل على حدوث العالم بطريقة الجمهور . هذا ما عندى فيه وأعود إلى النظر فيها في الكتاب
He says: The proof relied upon by the majority of theologians (mutakallimūn) in this issue needs to establish an argument for one of the four claims mentioned, which is the impossibility of the existence of events with no beginning in the past. So we first present what was said about it and against it, then I mention what I hold concerning it. I say: The philosophers (al-awā'il) said regarding the necessity of the finitude of past events: that since each one of them is originated, the whole is originated. Objection was raised against it that the judgment on the whole may differ from the judgment on the individuals. Then they said: increase and decrease apply to past events, so they are finite—yet God's objects of knowledge and powers are infinite. Then the investigators (al-muḥaṣṣilūn) among them said: Past events, when taken once starting from now going into the past, and another time starting from this time of last year going into the past, and one is applied to the other in imagination, by making the starting point one and both coincide in going into the past—their equality is impossible, otherwise the existence of events occurring in the time that is now (i.e., last year) and their non-existence would be the same, and it is impossible for the series starting from last year to exceed the series starting from now, because what is deducted from two equals is not greater than either of them. So it is necessary that the series starting from last year in the past direction be less than the series starting from now in that direction, and that can only happen if it ends before the end of the series starting from now, and the lesser is finite, and the greater added to it by a finite amount is finite, so the whole is finite. And the opponent's objection to them: that this application only occurs in imagination (al-wahm), and that requires the two applied things to be depicted in it, and the infinite is not depicted in imagination, and it is clear that they do not occur in existence, let alone imagining their application in existence—so this proof depends on something that does not occur either in imagination or in existence. Also, increase and decrease are only assumed in the finite end, not in the end about which the dispute concerns its finitude, so it has no effect on it. This is the sum of the discussion in this place. And I say: Every temporal thing (ḥādith) is described as being prior to what comes after it and being posterior to what comes before it, and the two considerations are different. So if we consider past events starting from now—once in terms of each one being prior, and once in terms of it being posterior—the priors and posteriors, which are different by consideration, coincide in existence, and their coincidence does not need imagining application. Nevertheless, it is necessary that the priors be more than the posteriors in the direction about which the dispute is, since the posteriors are finite in the past because they must end before the priors end, and the priors added to them by a finite amount are also finite. Thus the proof is completed, refuting the objection against it, and by that the proof of the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-‘ālam) is completed according to the method of the majority. This is what I hold concerning it, and I shall return to the examination of it in the book.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is the author's comprehensive treatment of the proof for the finitude of past events, which is a key argument for the temporality of the world. It begins with the philosophers' (al-awā'il) argument: since each event is originated, the whole chain must be originated; but an objection states that the whole may differ from the parts. Then they argue that increase and decrease apply to past events, so they must be finite—but they note that God's knowledge and power are infinite, so that counterexample does not apply. Then the 'investigators' give a more precise argument using the application (taṭbīq) of two series starting from different points in the past. The objection from opponents is that this application is merely imaginary and the infinite cannot be depicted in imagination. The author then gives his own solution: instead of relying on imaginary application, he uses the real relation of 'prior' and 'posterior' that every event has. By considering events both as prior (to later events) and as posterior (to earlier events), we get two sets that coincide in existence without needing imagination. The posteriors are necessarily finite because they stop (since each event is followed by another until the present), and the priors exceed them by a finite amount, making the whole finite. Thus, the objection is overcome, and the proof for the world's creation is sound.

Key Terms

تناهي
تطبيق
سابق
لاحق

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof for finitude of past events requires imagining an infinite series, which is impossible.
Correction: The author shows that by using the real relations of priority and posteriority, the proof does not rely on imagination; the series are finite based on their structure.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the objection that the application (taṭbīq) argument relies on imagination?
A) By denying imagination entirely
B) By using the real relations 'prior' and 'posterior' that exist in reality
C) By accepting the objection and giving a different proof
D) By claiming that imagination is valid for infinite sets

Key Takeaway
The author proves past events finite by using the real relations of priority and posteriority, avoiding reliance on imaginary application.

The author shows that past events are finite without relying on imagination. Consider your own past regrets or future worries: are they based on an imagined infinite sequence of problems, or can you see them as finite moments that have an end? How can understanding the finitude of events free you from being overwhelmed by your past or future?

In your own spiritual life, are you relying on imagined concepts of Allah's existence or on real, reasoned conviction? What step can you take to move from imagination to certainty in your faith?

Early Arguments for Finitude of Past Events
Al-Razi presents the early arguments and the opponent's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early kalam arguments for why past events must be finite, including the superimposition proof, and the main objection raised against it.

Core Idea
If past events were infinite, contradictory conclusions would follow — so they must be finite.

Imam al-Razi begins by presenting the arguments of the early theologians (al-awā'il) for the finitude of past events. These arguments rely on the nature of events as originated, the applicability of increase and decrease, and a sophisticated superimposition argument. However, opponents raised a critical objection that the superimposition only occurs in estimation, not reality. This section sets the stage for al-Razi's own resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the early arguments
Read the first set of arguments: (1) each event originated so whole originated, (2) increase/decrease applies, (3) the superimposition argument. Note the objection that the whole may differ from parts.
The analogical argument about God's knowledge and power is also relevant.
2
Understand the superimposition argument
This is the central proof: by comparing two sequences of past events starting from different points, the necessity of finitude is established. Grasp the logical steps.
The argument requires careful attention to the idea of 'starting from now' vs 'starting from last year'.
3
Examine the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that the superimposition is only imaginary, not real, and the infinite cannot be imagined. Also, increase/decrease is only on the finite side.
This objection sets up the need for al-Razi's refined approach.
4
Reflect on the dialectical style
Notice how the passage is structured as a debate: argument, objection, and then later a response. This is typical of kalam literature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأربع المذكورة وهو امتناع وجو حوادث لا أول لها في جانب الماضى فنورد أولا ماقيل فيه وعليه ثم أذكر ما عندى فيه فأقول. الأوائل قالوا في وجوب تناهى الحوادث الماضية: أنه لما كان كل واحد منها حادثا كان الكل حادثا. اعترض عليه بأن حكم الكل ربما مخالف الحكم على الآحاد. ثم قالوا الزيادة والنقصان بتطرة ان إلى الحوادث الماضية فتكون متناهية، وعرض معلومات الله تعالى ومقدوراته فإن الأولى أكثر من الثانية مع كونهما غير متناهين. ثم قال المحملون منهم الحوادث الماضية إذا أخذت تارة مبتدأة من الآن ذاهبة فى الماضى وتارة مبتدأة في مثل هذا الوقت من السنة الماضية ذاهبة في الماضى وأطبقت إحداهما على الأخرى فى النوهم، بأن يجعل المبدأ واحدا وهما في الذهاب إلى الماضى متطابقان استحال تساويهما، وإلا كان وجود الحوادث الواقعة في الزمان الذى هى الآن وهى السنة الماضية وعدمها واحدا، واستحال كون المبتدأة من السنة الماضية زائدة على المبتدأة من الآن لأن ما ينقص من المتساويين لا يكون زائدا على كل واحد منهما فإداً يجب أن يكون المبتدأة من السنة الماضية في جانب الماضى أنقص من المبتدأة الآن فى ذلك الجانب، ولا يمكن ذلك إلا بانتهائه قبل انتهاء المبتدأة من الآن ويكون الأنقص متناهيا والزائد عليه بمقدار متناه يكون متناعيا فيكون الكل متناهيا. واعتراض الخصم عليهم بأن هذا التطبيق لا يقع إلا فى الوهم وذلك يكون بشرط ارتسام المطابقين فيه وغير المتناهى لا يرتسم في الوهم، ومن البين أنهما لا يحصلان في الوجود مما فضلا عن توهم التطبيق فيهما في الوجود، فإذا هذا الدليل موقوف على حصول ما لا يحصل لا فى الوهم ولا فى الوجود وأيضاً الزيادة والنقصان إنما فرض في الطرف المتناهى لا فى الطرف الذى وقع النزاع فى تناديه فهو غير مؤثر فيه فهذا حاصل كلام، في هذا الموضع. وأنا أقول: إن كل حادث موصوف بكونه سابقا على ما بعده ويكون لاحقا بما قبله والاعتبار ان مختلفان، فإذا اعتبرنا الحوادث الماضية المبتدأة من الآن تارة من حيث كل واحد منها ساق وتارة من حيث هو بعينه لاحق كانت السوابق واللواحق المنباينين بالاعتبار متطابقين فى الوجود ولا يحتاج في تطابق هما إلى توهم تطبيق ومع ذلك يجب كون السوابق أكثر من اللواحق فى الجانب الذى وقع النزاع فيه، إذا اللواحق متناهية في الماضى لوجوب انقطاعها قبل انقطاع السوابق، والسوابق الزائدة عليها بمقدار متناه متناهية أيضاً، فإذا قد تم الدليل في سقوط ما اعترض عليه منه ويتم بذلك الدليل على حدوث العالم بطريقة الجمهور، هذا ما عندى فيه وأعود إلى النظر فيها في الكتاب.
The aforementioned four [issues], namely the impossibility of the existence of events without a beginning in the past. So we first present what was said for and against it, then I mention what I have regarding it, and I say: The earliest scholars said regarding the necessity of the finitude of past events: since each one of them is originated, the whole is originated. An objection was raised against them that the judgment of the whole may differ from the judgment of the individuals. Then they said: increase and decrease apply to past events, so they are finite. And the field of God's knowledge and His power: the former is greater than the latter despite both being infinite. Then the [proponents] among them said: past events if taken sometimes starting from now going into the past, and sometimes starting from the same time last year going into the past, and one is superimposed on the other in estimation, by making the beginning one and they are identical in going into the past, their equality is impossible, otherwise the existence of events occurring at the time that is now and last year and their non-existence would be one, and it is impossible for the starting point from last year to exceed the starting point from now because what is subtracted from two equals cannot be greater than each one. Therefore the starting point from last year must be less in the past direction than the starting point from now, and that is impossible unless it ends before the ending of the starting point from now, and the lesser is finite and the greater by a finite amount is finite, so the whole is finite. And the opponent's objection against them: this superimposition occurs only in estimation, and that requires the representation of the two superimposable objects in it, and the infinite is not represented in estimation, and it is obvious that they do not exist in reality, let alone the estimation of superimposition in existence. So this proof depends on the attainment of what is unattainable neither in estimation nor in existence. Also increase and decrease are assumed in the finite side, not in the side regarding which the dispute is about its finitude, so it is not effective in it. This is the gist of the discourse on this point. And I say: Every event is characterized by being prior to what comes after and posterior to what precedes it, and the two considerations are different. So if we consider past events starting from now, sometimes as each one being prior and sometimes as each one being posterior, the priors and posters differentiated by consideration are identical in existence, and their superimposition does not require estimation, yet the priors must be more than the posters in the direction under dispute, since the posters are finite in the past because they must end before the priors end, and the priors exceeding them by a finite amount are also finite. Thus the proof is complete in refuting the objection against it, and by that the proof for the temporality of the world is established according to the method of the majority. This is what I hold regarding it, and I return to the discussion in the book.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This long passage is a complete argument for the impossibility of an infinite regress of past events, leading to a proof that the world is temporally originated (hadith). It is structured as a dialectical exchange. First, the early scholars argue that since each past event is originated, the totality must be originated—but an objection notes that the whole may differ from its parts. Then they propose that increase and decrease apply to past events, so they must be finite; this is supported by an analogy with God's knowledge and power, where knowledge is greater yet both infinite, implying that past events are finite because they are subject to increase. The main proof involves superimposing two series of past events starting from different points, leading to a contradiction if they were infinite. The opponent objects that the superimposition is only imaginary and the infinite cannot be imagined; also that increase/decrease applies only to the finite side. Al-Razi then gives his own response: by considering each event as both prior and posterior (relative to others), the series of priors and posters are identical in existence but the priors must outnumber the posters in the past direction; since posters are finite (they must stop before priors), the priors—being finite in excess—also must be finite. Thus the regress is impossible, proving the world's temporal origination.

Key Terms
تناهى الحوادث
حوادث لا أول لها
التطبيق
الزيادة والنقصان

A) The whole may differ from its parts.
B) Superimposition only occurs in estimation, and the infinite cannot be imagined.
C) Increase and decrease do not apply to past events.
D) The two series are never actually equal.

Key Takeaway
The early theologians used the superimposition argument to show past events must be finite, but opponents objected that it relies on imagination, not reality.

?
Al-Razi records that the superimposition argument depends on estimation (wahm), not reality. In what area of your own beliefs might you be mistaking an imagined construct for a proven truth? Will you re-examine that belief today, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
The early scholars presented proofs, but opponents objected that the proof rests on unattainable assumptions. How often do you critically examine the foundations of your own theological positions? Commit to one assumption you will test against the primary sources this week.

Al-Razi's Refined Argument for Finitude
Al-Razi presents his own argument for finitude using priority and posteriority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's own response to the objection, which uses the concepts of priority and posteriority to prove the finitude of past events and the origination of the world.

Core Idea
By considering each event as both prior and posterior, the necessity of finitude becomes clear without relying on imagination.

After presenting the early arguments and their objection, Imam al-Razi provides his own solution. He observes that each event is both prior (to later events) and posterior (to earlier events). Considering these two aspects separately, the series of priors and posters are identical in existence but the priors must outnumber the posters in the past direction. Since the posters are finite, the priors—and hence all past events—must also be finite. This elegantly avoids the opponent's objection and establishes a sound proof for the world's temporal origination.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dual aspect of events
Al-Razi says every event is described as prior (sābiq) to what comes after and posterior (lāhiq) to what precedes. These are two different considerations (iʿtibārān).
This is the key insight.
2
Apply the two considerations to the series
Consider past events from now: if we take each event as prior, we get one series; if as posterior, another. These two series are the same events but considered from different angles.
They are identical in existence (mutābiqān fī l-wujūd).
3
Compare the numbers of priors and posters
Since each posterior must be preceded by a prior, the number of priors in the past must be greater than the number of posters. But the posters are finite because they must end before the priors end (since a posterior requires a prior before it). Therefore the priors are also finite, and thus all past events are finite.
The opponent's objection about imagination is irrelevant because this reasoning uses actual existence, not mental superimposition.
4
Conclude the proof for the world's temporality
Al-Razi states that this completes the proof for the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) according to the method of the majority.
This is a major conclusion in kalam.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument for finitude of past events depends on imagining infinite series and superimposing them in the mind.

?

?

If your existence is borrowed from each prior moment, what have you done today to acknowledge the One who holds your existence from moment to moment?
?

Razi's Argument and the Philosophers' Proofs
Razi states his own argument and enumerates three philosophical proofs for the world's eternity without immediate refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's central argument that the world is not necessarily eternal because what was not eternal in the past remains receptive to non-existence, and you will be able to identify the three key philosophical proofs for the world's eternity that he sets out to refute.

Core Idea
If the world was not eternal in the past, its essence is always receptive to non-existence.

Razi begins by presenting his own concise argument against the world's necessary eternity, then lays out the three main proofs the philosophers use to argue for its eternity. Though his refutations are not included here, understanding these proofs is essential for appreciating the full debate.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's own argument
Start by reading the opening lines where Razi states his position: whatever was not eternal in the past (azalī) cannot be eternal in the future (abadī) because its essence is inherently receptive to non-existence, a permanent concomitant.
Notice the causal link: 'lack of azalī' implies 'receptivity to non-existence' implies 'not abadī'.
2
Examine the first philosophical proof: God as Necessitating Cause
The philosophers argue that since God (the efficient cause) is a necessitating cause by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), His own eternity necessitates the eternity of the world.
This is the famous 'emanation' argument: if cause is eternal, effect must be eternal.
3
Examine the second proof: Impossibility of Time's Annihilation
They argue that if time were to cease after existing, its non-existence would be after its existence in time, creating a contradiction: time would exist at the moment it is assumed non-existent.
This is a logical puzzle about temporal asymmetry.
4
Examine the third proof: Necessity of a Substrate (Hayula)
Everything that can cease to exist requires a substrate (hayula) to sustain the possibility of its non-existence. If the world could cease, it would need an eternal matter that never ceases.
This leads to the conclusion that matter (hayula) is eternal and never without form, so body cannot become non-existent.

Source Text

مسألة : العالم لا يجب أن يكون أبديا خلافا للفلاسفة والكرامية لنا أن ما لم يكن أزليا وجب أن لا يكون أبديا لأن ما لا يكون أزليا كانت ماهيته قابلة للعدم وذلك القبول لا يتقرر مع من لوازم تلك الماهية فكون A question: The world is not necessarily eternal, contrary to the philosophers and the Karramiyya. For we [argue]: that which was not eternal must not be eternal, because whatever was not eternal, its essence was receptive to non-existence, and that receptivity is not [merely] concomitant with that essence? Rather, it is from the necessary concomitants of that essence, so the essence is always receptive to non-existence. As for the philosophers, they adduced several [arguments]: first, that the efficient cause of the world is necessitating by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), so the permanence of [the cause] entails the permanence of the world. Second, that if time were annihilated, its non-existence after its existence would be posterior in time, so time would exist at the moment it is assumed non-existent, which is a contradiction. Third, that everything that admits non-existence, its possibility of non-existence is present before its non-existence, and that possibility must have a locus (mahall), i.e., there must be something that is described by that non-existence, and that is not the existence of the thing because that which can be described by something must be established with that thing, and the existence of that thing is its non-existence. So there must be another thing that sustains the possibility of its non-existence, and that is the matter (hayula). Thus everything that admits non-existence has a matter; if non-existence were admitted for the matter, it would require another matter ad infinitum. Therefore matter does not admit non-existence, and it has been established that matter is never free of corporeal form, so the non-existence of body is impossible. The Karramiyya Muslims argued for the necessity of the world's eternity [as follows]: the non-existence of the world after its existence must be either by the annihilating act of an annihilator, or by the advent of an opposite, or by the removal of a condition. All three divisions are invalid, so the claim of the world's non-existence after its existence is invalid. As for [the first], we say that it is not permissible for it to be annihilated by an annihilation act because if the annihilation is an existent thing, then that existence is not identical with the non-existence of the world, otherwise existence would be identical with non-existence. Rather, its utmost is the substance? That would be annihilation by opposite, and this is not this division but the second. And if it is not existent, it is pure non-existence, so it is impossible to attribute it to an agent, because reason makes no distinction between saying 'He did nothing at all' and saying 'He produced non-existence.' Otherwise, one non-existence would differ from another, so each non-existence would have change and stability, so non-existence would be something positive, which is a contradiction. And as for [the second], we say it is not permissible for it to be annihilated by the advent of an opposite for two reasons. First: the advent of the opposite depends on the removal of the other opposite, so if the removal of the other opposite were caused by the advent of this opposite, a circularity would arise, which is impossible. [Second]: opposition exists on both sides, and the removal of one is no more likely than the reversal. So either each of them is removed by the other, which is impossible because the efficient cause of the non-existence of each is the existence of the other, and the efficient cause is simultaneous with the effect, so if both effects occurred together, both existences would occur together, thus they would be existent and non-existent simultaneously, which is impossible. Or neither is removed by the other, so the two opposites would coexist. It cannot be said: the newly originated is stronger than the remaining because the newly originated at the moment of its origination is attached to [the eternal?] and the remaining is not so, and because the newly originated...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a new mas'ala (question) in which Razi asserts that the world is not necessarily eternal (abadī), contrary to the philosophers and the Karramiyya. He first gives his own argument: whatever is not eternal in the past (azalī) must also not be eternal in the future, because its essence is inherently receptive to non-existence, and this receptivity is a permanent concomitant of that essence. Then he refutes three proofs from the philosophers: (1) if God is a necessitating cause (mujib bi al-dhat), His eternity would entail the world's eternity; (2) the impossibility of time's annihilation because its non-existence would be after its existence, requiring time to exist at the moment of assumed non-existence; (3) the need for a substrate (hayula) to sustain the possibility of non-existence, leading to the conclusion that matter is eternal and never lacking form, so body cannot cease. After this, Razi addresses the Karramiyya—a sect that apparently affirmed the world's eternity. They argued that the world's non-existence after existence could only occur via an external agent's act, a contrary's advent, or removal of a condition; all three being impossible. Razi refutes each: (a) an annihilating act cannot be existent (because then it would be identical with what it destroys) or non-existent (because pure non-existence cannot be attributed to an agent); (b) the advent of a contrary leads to circularity or simultaneous existence of opposites; (c) the removal of a condition is also problematic. The passage ends with an objection ('It cannot be said...') that is cut off, likely continuing the refutation. The overall structure is: Razi states his position, presents the opponents' arguments, and then refutes them systematically.

Key Terms
azali
abadi
mujib bi al-dhat
hayula

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi's own argument, what is the relationship between something not being eternal in the past (azalī) and its being eternal in the future (abadī)?
A) If something is not azalī, it must be abadī.
B) If something is not azalī, it cannot be abadī.
C) If something is azalī, it cannot be abadī.
D) There is no relationship between azalī and abadī.

Key Takeaway
Razi's own argument is that receptivity to non-existence is a permanent concomitant of an essence that was not eternal, so it cannot be eternal in the future; the philosophers' three proofs relate to God's nature, time, and matter, and must be refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every created thing is, by its very essence, always receptive to non-existence, then what truly anchors your sense of security? Are you building your life on things that could vanish at any moment, or on the One who alone is self-subsistent?
?
The philosophers argued that God's nature forces Him to create eternally. Do you ever treat your relationship with God as a mechanical necessity—expecting blessings based on your actions—rather than a living, sovereign relationship where He acts by will?
?
Razi states that the world is not necessarily eternal, meaning it had a beginning and could have an end. If you truly believed that this world is fleeting and your own existence is contingent, what would you do differently starting today?

Allah's Existence

8 min
Refuting the Karramiyya's Argument for the World's Eternity
Razi refutes the Karramiyya's argument by dismantling their threefold division of possible causes of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya's threefold argument for the world's necessary eternity and Razi's systematic refutation of each division, including the logical problems of annihilation by act, advent of opposite, and removal of condition.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya's exhaustive argument is dismantled by showing each option leads to absurdity.

After presenting the philosophers' proofs, Razi turns to the Karramiyya, who argued that the world's non-existence after existence is impossible based on an exhaustive division of possible causes. Razi refutes each division in detail, revealing logical contradictions and circularities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Karramiyya's three divisions
The Karramiyya claim that the world's non-existence after existence can only occur via: (a) an act of an external annihilator, (b) the advent of an opposite, or (c) the removal of a condition. They argue all three are impossible.
This is a classic trilemma often used by mutakallimun.
2
Examine Razi's refutation of the first division (annihilation by act)
Razi argues that if the annihilating act is an existing thing, it cannot be identical with the non-existence it produces; if it is non-existent, it cannot be attributed to an agent because pure non-existence cannot be an act.
He also points out that if the act were existent, it would be an opposite, falling into the second division.
3
Examine Razi's refutation of the second division (advent of opposite)
Razi gives two reasons: (1) the advent of an opposite depends on the removal of the other opposite, leading to circularity (dawr); (2) opposition is symmetrical, so either each opposite removes the other (impossible) or neither does (coexistence of opposites).
He also rejects the objection that the newly originated is 'stronger' than the remaining.

Commentary
This passage is a new mas'ala (question) in which Razi asserts that the world is not necessarily eternal (abadī), contrary to the philosophers and the Karramiyya. He first gives his own argument: whatever is not eternal in the past (azalī) must also not be eternal in the future, because its essence is inherently receptive to non-existence, and this receptivity is a permanent concomitant of that essence. The passage ends with an objection ('It cannot be said...') that is cut off, likely continuing the refutation. Key Terms
isnad
taqabul
dawr
'adam

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Karramiyya's exhaustive division is logically sound and covers all possibilities for the world's non-existence.
Correction: Razi shows that the first division collapses into the second and that the second leads to circularity or simultaneous opposites, so the division is not genuinely exhaustive or coherent.

Checkpoint
In refuting the second division (advent of opposite), what is Razi's first reason?
A) The advent of an opposite depends on the removal of the other opposite, leading to circularity.
B) The advent of an opposite is impossible because it would require time to exist.
C) The advent of an opposite is impossible because it would require a necessary being.
D) The advent of an opposite is not a real cause.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya's claim that the world must be eternal because none of the three possible causes of non-existence can occur is refuted by showing that each supposed cause either self-destructs or reduces to another problematic division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because you cannot conceive of a thing, it cannot be? How does this passage expose the limitations of human logic when applied to divine action? What change in your attitude towards God's power is required?
?
The Karramiyya attempted to prove the world eternal using reason. Where in your own life have you tried to confine God's decrees within your own logical expectations? How can you actively submit to God's absolute freedom to create and annihilate as He wills?
?
Razi argues that non-existence cannot be caused by an efficient cause. Do you ever treat God's non-action as if it were a kind of action? How might this affect your understanding of divine wisdom in allowing things to cease? What does it mean to trust that God's withholding of existence is not a 'doing' but a wise refrain?

Philosophers' Proof: Celestial Bodies are Simple, Spherical, and Moved by Will
Philosophers' argument for the simplicity and spherical shape of celestial bodies and their voluntary motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that celestial bodies are simple, spherical, and move by a voluntary will, not by nature or force.

Core Idea
Celestial bodies move voluntarily because they are neither driven by nature nor forced.

The philosophers present an intricate argument to establish the nature of the heavenly spheres. They begin by showing that direction exists and is indivisible, leading to a spherical determiner that defines above and below. From its properties, they deduce that its motion cannot be natural or forced, hence it must be voluntary. This passage reveals the rational framework behind their view of the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the passage from 'أما الأجسام الفلكية' to the end of the argument. Notice how each claim builds on the previous one.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Understand direction (al-jiha)
See why the philosophers claim direction must exist and be indivisible. Follow the reasoning about the mover and the limit.
3
Follow the spherical determiner
From the existence of direction, they deduce a spherical determiner (muhaddad kuri) that defines above and below by its center and circumference.
4
Grasp simplicity and motion
Understand why the determiner is simple (not composed) and why it cannot have rectilinear motion, leading to its circular motion.
5
Analyze the nature of motion
Consider why the motion cannot be natural or forced, leading to the conclusion that it is voluntary, making the heaven a living being.
